## Solvency - Defense

### 1NC \-- Solvency -- Petro States

#### Norway & Finland security perceptions undermine transition

Marja Helena **Sivonen**, 20**25** **SYKE** Finnish Environment
Institute, Climate Solutions Unit - University of Tampere, Faculty of
Social Sciences, & Paula Kivimaa - SPRU, University of Sussex
\"Interrelations between security and the zero‑carbon energy transition
in the Finnish and Norwegian Arctic.\" Energy Research & Social Science
119 (2025): 103841.
<https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S2214629624004328>
(ermo-MSDI, acc 6-22-2025)

And Norway, Sweden, and Finland are on different sides of the table in
that thinking; of course, in the words of the politicians, they say "oh
yeah, we need to support the green shift and we need to do that" but
[[everyone knows that Norway\'s main focus is still oil and gas
drilling]{.underline}]{.mark}. I mean, that\'s the reason why we are
millionaires.... I used to say that [[the Norwegians are petroholics. We
know that it is bad, but we can\'t resist]{.underline}]{.mark}. We just
need one more drilling, one more oil platform. "No, yeah, put it away",
like alcohol, you know. Just one more. (Interview, Norway other)

[[The Finnish strategies]{.underline}]{.mark} discussed continued fossil
fuel production in Norway. The plans [[were to provide expertise in
cold-climate operations and business for the oil and gas sector,
supporting]{.underline}]{.mark} the regime in [[both
Russia]{.underline}]{.mark} (prior to 2022) [[and
Norway]{.underline}]{.mark}, an issue that was also mentioned in the
interviews. Although we detected only a few direct references in the
strategies to keeping the Arctic area inhabited explicitly through these
plans, it is reasonable to assume that one way to gain positive security
was to connect regime support and unsustainable energy production to
security justifications, even in Finland, which has no oil and gas
production.

#### SQ Norway strategy is JOINT production

Marja Helena **Sivonen**, 20**25** **SYKE** Finnish Environment
Institute, Climate Solutions Unit - University of Tampere, Faculty of
Social Sciences, & Paula Kivimaa - SPRU, University of Sussex
\"Interrelations between security and the zero‑carbon energy transition
in the Finnish and Norwegian Arctic.\" Energy Research & Social Science
119 (2025): 103841.
<https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S2214629624004328>
(ermo-MSDI, acc 6-22-2025)

5.3. Regime support

[[In the Norwegian strategies, the electricity transmission network
development was connected to new petroleum and other business activity
in the High North, suggesting support for rather than the decline of the
regime]{.underline}]{.mark}. The 2011 strategy posited [[energy as a
significant part of future efforts to develop Arctic
areas]{.underline}]{.mark}; this [[would be primarily oil and
gas]{.underline}]{.mark}, but there would also be renewable energy -- an
example of positive security thinking, as the developing energy sector
would boost population growth and create prosperity beyond the energy
sector. These views were echoed in the interviews, with an
acknowledgment of the negative effects of climate change that were
viewed as jeopardising positive development.

[[The Norwegian strategies pointed out that the renewable energy sector
of the northern areas would not be developed on its own. Rather, it
would be developed **in connection to fossil fuel**
production]{.underline}]{.mark}. However, [[the
strategies]{.underline}]{.mark} throughout frame the production of
fossil fuels as according with sustainable development goals. They [[do
not consider the whole chain from production to end consumption and the
emissions of usage]{.underline}]{.mark}, thus only allowing discussion
at a broad and partial level. One niche beyond fossil fuel production to
be developed is bioenergy from algae. However, the 2009 strategy did not
consider its potential to be strong, thus, managing expectations already
in the beginning. Although [[hydropower]{.underline}]{.mark} can be
understood as part of the regime due to its long history and abundant
energy production, it [[too was harnessed to support plans for fossil
fuel energy production]{.underline}]{.mark}.

#### Arctic petro states disrupt climate transition

Marja Helena **Sivonen**, 20**25** **SYKE** Finnish Environment
Institute, Climate Solutions Unit - University of Tampere, Faculty of
Social Sciences, & Paula Kivimaa - SPRU, University of Sussex
\"Interrelations between security and the zero‑carbon energy transition
in the Finnish and Norwegian Arctic.\" Energy Research & Social Science
119 (2025): 103841.
<https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S2214629624004328>
(ermo-MSDI, acc 6-22-2025)

We used transformative outcomes (TOs): the three macro-processes and 12
sub-processes \[19\] for data analysis. However, the analysis showed
that, in the European Arctic context, the findings could mainly be
presented in the level of macro-processes (merging niche development
with niche expansion and regime decline). [[Due to the strong support
given still to fossil fuels in the region, we added a category of regime
support]{.underline}]{.mark} to the analysis. Previous research on
[[Eurasian Arctic regimes has shown that the state as a regulatory and
funding body is so closely connected to fossil fuel production in the
area that powerful pressure from the regime has facilitated continued
action]{.underline}]{.mark} \[2,3\].

### 1NC \-- Solvency -- No Governance

#### AC fails -- Ukraine tensions

Marja Helena **Sivonen**, 20**25** **SYKE** Finnish Environment
Institute, Climate Solutions Unit - University of Tampere, Faculty of
Social Sciences, & Paula Kivimaa - SPRU, University of Sussex
\"Interrelations between security and the zero‑carbon energy transition
in the Finnish and Norwegian Arctic.\" Energy Research & Social Science
119 (2025): 103841.
<https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S2214629624004328>
(ermo-MSDI, acc 6-22-2025)

In the Finnish interviews, [[the EU\'s call to ban fossil fuel
production in the Arctic was frequently mentioned]{.underline}]{.mark}.
Although [[many described it as **inefficient**, suggesting its status
as **merely ceremonial** and yet to be determined in practice, the
possibility was regarded as an important opening]{.underline}]{.mark}.
This can be interpreted as an attempt to change perceptions of the
seriousness of climate change. [[The Arctic
Council]{.underline}]{.mark}, an intergovernmental forum promoting
cooperation in the Arctic, [[was discussed as a good
structure]{.underline}]{.mark} for monitoring and knowledge
dissemination in climate change mitigation and adaptation. While the
Arctic Council is not a security actor, this can be interpreted as
providing positive security by offering a platform to broaden the
epistemological foundation on which discussions are based, although the
Council officially does not deal with issues of negative security.
[[Arctic cooperation was seen as a major challenge due to **Russia\'s
war in Ukraine**, which has created palpable military security
**pressure** on energy transitions and sustainable
development]{.underline}]{.mark}. However, its actual effectiveness in
relation to the transition was questioned: "[[The Arctic Council is a
**paper tiger** on this issue.... Oil and gas drilling are and have
always been part of their inventories, and the academic
community]{.underline}]{.mark} is doing a good job, but this [[has had
**no effect** on decision-making]{.underline}]{.mark}" (Interview,
Finland, researcher).

#### Arctic cooperation improbable given diverging interests

Ishfaq Hussain **Malik** 20**25** (University of **Leeds,**
Post-Doctoral Research Fellow at the School of **Geography**,) also
Rayees Ahmed (Divecha Centre for Climate Change, Indian Institute of
Science) James D. Ford (Priestley Centre for Climate Futures, University
of Leeds) Abdur Rahim Hamidi (Sustainability Research Institute, School
of Earth and Environment, University of Leeds) \"Arctic Warming:
Cascading Climate Impacts and Global Consequences.\" Climate 13.5
(2025): 85. https://www.mdpi.com/2225-1154/13/5/85 (ermo-MSDI, acc
6-23-2025)

The susceptibility of the Arctic to climate change has led to several
international efforts aimed at encouraging international cooperation,
although the efficacy of these efforts varies \[242\]. [[The Arctic
Council]{.underline}]{.mark}, formed in 1996, [[is the
premier]{.underline}]{.mark} intergovernmental
[[platform]{.underline}]{.mark} for Arctic governments and Indigenous
communities \[243,244\]. It has played an important role in producing
critical reports, such as the Arctic Climate Impact Assessment (ACIA),
which highlights the multifaceted effects of warming on the region
\[245\]. The Council supports research and sustainable resource use
through initiatives, such as the Agreement on Enhancing International
Arctic Scientific Cooperation (2017) and the Agreement to Prevent
Unregulated Fishing in the Central Arctic Ocean (2018) \[75\].
[[However, its inability to impose legally binding controls on carbon
emissions or industrial activity limits its overall
effectiveness]{.underline}]{.mark}. The Paris Agreement (2015), while
global in scope, indirectly addresses Arctic warming by aiming to keep
the global temperature rise to well below 2 °C \[246\]. Arctic-specific
solutions, such as enhanced financial support for adaptation in polar
regions or tighter timelines for emissions reductions in high-latitude
countries, are noticeably lacking \[247\]. Similarly, the Polar Code,
produced by the International Maritime Organisation, establishes rules
for Arctic shipping but calls for broader coverage to offset the hazards
of increased commercial activity, such as stronger limits on black
carbon emissions and ballast water discharge \[248\]. [[The **lack** of
comprehensive agreements explicitly designed to **address** Arctic
concerns, such as]{.underline}]{.mark} regulating [[oil and
gas]{.underline}]{.mark} development [[or]{.underline}]{.mark}
overseeing [[geoengineering]{.underline}]{.mark} experiments,
[[emphasises the need for more international action. Proposals for a
binding]{.underline}]{.mark} Arctic Climate
[[Treaty]{.underline}]{.mark}, similar to the Antarctic Treaty System,
[[remain contentious due to **competing interests among** Arctic states
and industry]{.underline}]{.mark} \[249\].

#### Governance frameworks are insufficient to address competing arctic interests

Ishfaq Hussain **Malik** 20**25** (University of **Leeds,**
Post-Doctoral Research Fellow at the School of **Geography**,) also
Rayees Ahmed (Divecha Centre for Climate Change, Indian Institute of
Science) James D. Ford (Priestley Centre for Climate Futures, University
of Leeds) Abdur Rahim Hamidi (Sustainability Research Institute, School
of Earth and Environment, University of Leeds) \"Arctic Warming:
Cascading Climate Impacts and Global Consequences.\" Climate 13.5
(2025): 85. https://www.mdpi.com/2225-1154/13/5/85 (ermo-MSDI, acc
6-23-2025)

Climate change is causing significant environmental and socioeconomic
changes in the Arctic, necessitating the implementation of strong
governance frameworks and adaptation strategies. The combination of
global climate dynamics, Indigenous knowledge systems, and geopolitical
interests has made the Arctic a focus for scientific research and policy
intervention. [[The Arctic Council serves as a major forum for
collaboration]{.underline}]{.mark} among Arctic nations and Indigenous
organisations \[266\]. It has played an important role in addressing
concerns, such as environmental preservation and sustainable
development, but its non-binding agreements impede enforcement \[32\].
[[Despite these efforts, gaps exist in the management of challenges,
such as increased shipping, resource extraction, and environmental
deterioration]{.underline}]{.mark}. For example, the [[rapid
melting]{.underline}]{.mark} of sea ice [[has increased geopolitical
competition]{.underline}]{.mark} for shipping routes and mineral
resources, [[emphasising the need for stronger legal
frameworks]{.underline}]{.mark} \[36,50,267\]. [[International
agreements]{.underline}]{.mark}, such as the United Nations Convention
on the Law of the Sea, [[provide legal processes for settling
disputes]{.underline}]{.mark}, particularly those involving marine
boundaries \[268\]. [[However]{.underline}]{.mark}, there are concerns
that [[these frameworks are insufficient to handle the Arctic's rapid
environmental changes]{.underline}]{.mark} \[212\]. The Arctic Fisheries
Agreement of 2018, which outlaws unregulated fishing in the central
Arctic Ocean, is a recent example of effective international cooperation
\[269\].

Institute, Climate Solutions Unit - University of Tampere, Faculty of

\"Interrelations between security and the zero‑carbon energy transition
in the Finnish and Norwegian Arctic.\" Energy Research & Social Science
119 (2025): 103841.
<https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S2214629624004328>
(ermo-MSDI, acc 6-22-2025)

[[Regime support was scarcely discussed]{.underline}]{.mark} in the
interviews, [[although several participants mentioned the improbability
of simply halting oil and gas production due to the economic dependence
of]{.underline}]{.mark} four Arctic states: [[Russia, the United States,
Canada and Norway]{.underline}]{.mark}. As the interviews were conducted
[[after Russia\'s invasion of Ukraine, this was directly connected to
hard security issues of protecting the state]{.underline}]{.mark}, its
assets and its claims of sovereignty over maritime areas. [[This
highlights the importance of more diverse paradigms that the framing of
positive security allows]{.underline}]{.mark}.

### 1NC \-- Solvency -- No Modeling

#### No solvency -- indigenous knowledge does not spill up

Ishfaq Hussain **Malik** 20**25** (University of **Leeds,**
Post-Doctoral Research Fellow at the School of **Geography**,) also
Rayees Ahmed (Divecha Centre for Climate Change, Indian Institute of
Science) James D. Ford (Priestley Centre for Climate Futures, University
of Leeds) Abdur Rahim Hamidi (Sustainability Research Institute, School
of Earth and Environment, University of Leeds) \"Arctic Warming:
Cascading Climate Impacts and Global Consequences.\" Climate 13.5
(2025): 85. https://www.mdpi.com/2225-1154/13/5/85 (ermo-MSDI, acc
6-23-2025)

The [[Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark} has several [[research
gaps]{.underline}]{.mark} that [[limit effective climate action
and]{.underline}]{.mark} equitable [[policy
development]{.underline}]{.mark}, particularly for Indigenous Peoples
and the ecosystem. [[An important gap is the **poor inclusion** of
Indigenous knowledge systems into **larger** scientific
frameworks]{.underline}]{.mark}. [[Indigenous]{.underline}]{.mark}
communities have built up extensive, [[place-based
knowledge]{.underline}]{.mark} about Arctic ecosystems over generations,
which [[is underutilised in scientific and policy
discussions]{.underline}]{.mark} \[285\]. The lack of
[[this]{.underline}]{.mark} knowledge [[reduces the cultural
significance and efficacy of adaptation strategies]{.underline}]{.mark},
particularly those aimed at traditional livelihoods, like hunting,
fishing, and reindeer herding. The detailed overview of the key research
gaps identified in this study is given in Supplementary Table S1.
Studies that combine Indigenous knowledge with science are critical for
developing inclusive policies that empower communities and strengthen
their resilience. Beyond Indigenous-specific gaps, larger ecological and
socioeconomic uncertainties persist. There is a lack of understanding
about Arctic marine ecosystems, specifically the combined effects of
ocean acidification, changing sea ice dynamics, and altered food webs.
Polar bears, walruses, and Arctic cod are among the species at risk due
to these conditions, which threaten both ecological equilibrium and
Indigenous sustenance. Economic gaps include a lack of comprehensive
appraisals of growing industries, such as Arctic shipping and resource
extraction. The effects of infrastructure expansion on vulnerable
permafrost and ecosystems are poorly understood, [[leaving important
concerns unanswered]{.underline}]{.mark}. These research gaps and future
goals highlight the need for a comprehensive and collaborative approach
to Arctic climate research, which ensures that scientific understanding
keeps up with the rapid changes taking place in this crucial area.
[[Addressing these gaps necessitates]{.underline}]{.mark}
interdisciplinary research, [[more collaboration]{.underline}]{.mark}
between Arctic and non-Arctic nations, [[and a focus on participatory
techniques]{.underline}]{.mark} that incorporate Indigenous perspectives
at all stages of development. By addressing these research gaps, the
Arctic can serve as a paradigm for integrated, sustainable, and
inclusive climate response.

Despite growing interest in Arctic climate change, several key research
gaps remain. These include limited high-resolution data across space and
seasons, insufficient integration of interacting climate drivers (e.g.,
greenhouse gases, albedo, and atmospheric dynamics), and unclear
linkages between Arctic warming and mid-latitude weather extremes.
Moreover, [[local-scale impacts on ecosystems and Indigenous communities
are underexplored, and observational
data]{.underline}]{.mark}---especially in remote and winter-dominated
regions---[[remain sparse]{.underline}]{.mark}. This study contributes
by synthesising multiple drivers and highlighting the Arctic's role as a
critical zone of interconnected global change.

#### No solvency without modeling

Kimberley **Miner**, 20**22** (Jet Propulsion Laboratory, **CIT** -
California Institute of Technology) ALSO - Merritt R. Turetsky 2, Edward
Malina1, Annett Bartsch3,4, Johanna Tamminen5, A. David McGuire6,
Andreas Fix7, Colm Sweeney8, Clayton D. Elder1 and Charles E. Miller 1
\"Permafrost carbon emissions in a changing Arctic.\" Nature Reviews
Earth & Environment 3.1 (2022): 55-67.
https://par.nsf.gov/servlets/purl/10313977 (ermo-MSDI, acc 6-23-2025)

[[To truly capture Arctic thaw dynamics, system models must
**integrate** scaled data **between** land surface]{.underline}]{.mark}
observations [[and terrain-level]{.underline}]{.mark} permafrost
[[models]{.underline}]{.mark}149--152. Since [[carbon partitioning
depends on]{.underline}]{.mark} numerous environmental factors,
[[**iterative** system models]{.underline}]{.mark} incorporating
vegetation sensitivity, the physical dynamics driving permafrost
degradation, hydrologic cycling and microbial transformation must all be
included16,72,76. [[Whether]{.underline}]{.mark} and to what extent the
[[permafrost]{.underline}]{.mark} landscape, already interspersed with
thaw lakes and thermokarst, [[transforms from a carbon sink into a
carbon source]{.underline}]{.mark} in a warmer world [[can **only** be
answered with combined modelling and observational
data]{.underline}]{.mark}40.

### 1NC \-- Solvency -- RE Fails

#### Government control undermines arctic RE

Gwen **Holdmann**, 20**25**, Alaska Center for Energy and Power,
**U**niversity of **Alaska** Fairbanks and Richard Wies Electrical and
Computer Engineering Department, College of Engineering and Mines,
University of Alaska Fairbanks, \"Harnessing the Arctic Wind: How Alaska
became a leader in wind energy microgrids.\" IEEE Electrification
Magazine 13.2 (2025): 6-15.
https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/abstract/document/11023057 (ermo-MSDI, acc
6-23-2025)

These [[innovations did not stem from a single
policy]{.underline}]{.mark} shift or technological breakthrough.
Instead, [[Alaska's rapid adoption of wind energy resulted from a
convergence of economic]{.underline}]{.mark} pressures,
[[technological]{.underline}]{.mark} advancements,
[[local]{.underline}]{.mark} ingenuity, [[and
sustained]{.underline}]{.mark} public [[investment]{.underline}]{.mark}.
This article explores how Alaska became a leader in wind--diesel
microgrids, the challenges that remain in scaling these solutions, and
the lessons Alaska offers to other remote communities.

Context: Why Alaska?

Alaska's extreme conditions (harsh weather, logistical challenges, and
high fuel costs) have created strong incentives to find ways to reduce
reliance on diesel. These challenges are not unique to Alaska; [[many
remote Arctic communities]{.underline}]{.mark} and islanded nations in
non-Arctic locations also [[rely on diesel. Yet, Alaska has emerged as a
leader]{.underline}]{.mark} in pioneering wind--diesel microgrid
solutions. Several factors have contributed to this outcome:

x Economic pressures as a catalyst for innovation: The high cost of
imported fuel has driven utilities and policy makers to explore
alternatives. In 2023, rural Alaska communities paid an average of
US\$0.55/kWh, with many residents facing a severe energy burden. In this
high-cost environment, [[even modest diesel
displacement]{.underline}]{.mark} with wind energy [[can
generate]{.underline}]{.mark} significant savings, making [[renewable
integration]{.underline}]{.mark} economically viable [[without
additional subsidies]{.underline}]{.mark}.

x Institutional and policy support: Alaska's energy landscape has
facilitated innovation in several ways. [[A largely deregulated utility
industry allows for swift, locally driven decision
making]{.underline}]{.mark}. Additionally, key state programs such as
the Renewable Energy Fund (REF) helped derisk early investments in wind
power, and research centers such as the Alaska Center for Energy and
Power provide technical support to communities.

x [[A culture of pragmatic experimentation]{.underline}]{.mark}: Early
adopters of wind--diesel microgrids in Alaska could not afford to wait
for fully developed solutions. Utilities and communities had to test,
refine, and sometimes discard technologies that failed in Arctic
conditions. This iterative approach to problem solving, akin to an agile
energy transition, allowed Alaska's microgrids to evolve rapidly.

#### EVT may not reduce CEM -- depends on demand offset questions

Mansoor **Khan**, 20**25**, 1 School of Intelligent Manufacturing and
Control **Engineering**, **Qilu Institute** of Technology, Jinan, China
ALSO - Hazrat Hassan ,2 Cai Li,3 Agyemang Kwasi Sampene ,3 and Francis
Kyere \"Achieving Carbon Neutrality Through Renewable Energy Transition
Amidst Economic Policy Uncertainty in the Arctic Region.\" International
Journal of Energy Research 2025.1 (2025): 2408883.
https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/pdf/10.1155/er/2408883 (ermo-MSDI,
acc 6-23-2025) KHAN ACRONYMS carbon emission (CEM) environmental
technology (EVT) economic policy uncertainty (EPU) renewable energy
transition (RET) environmental governance (EVG) Rapid urbanization
transition (URT)

Moreover, [[environmental technology]{.underline}]{.mark}
([EVT]{.underline}) is essential to environmental quality and is
strategic in economic transformation and development. Technological
advancements during the industrial revolution have changed economies,
improved the processing of already-produced goods, encouraged RET, and
expanded energy efficiency, ultimately improving environmental quality
\[18\]. Moreover, prior studies have argued that the [[fast
integration]{.underline}]{.mark} of EVT [[can help]{.underline}]{.mark}
monitor and mitigate CEM \[19, 20\]. Nevertheless,
[[other]{.underline}]{.mark} extant [[analyses have demonstrated that
EVT increases enterprises' **demand** for power
consumption]{.underline}]{.mark} to run operations. These studies also
argue that [[advanced EVT]{.underline}]{.mark} [[is]{.underline}]{.mark}
heavy, [[highly ene**rgy intensive**, and adversary contributes to
lessening CEM]{.underline}]{.mark} \[21, 22\]. This evidence has
demonstrated that [[the connection between EVT and CEM is still
**unclear**]{.underline}]{.mark} and requires further analysis.

#### NEG - Arctic RE should vary by the country & region

Mansoor **Khan**, 20**25**, 1 School of Intelligent Manufacturing and
Control **Engineering**, **Qilu Institute** of Technology, Jinan, China
ALSO - Hazrat Hassan ,2 Cai Li,3 Agyemang Kwasi Sampene ,3 and Francis
Kyere \"Achieving Carbon Neutrality Through Renewable Energy Transition
Amidst Economic Policy Uncertainty in the Arctic Region.\" International
Journal of Energy Research 2025.1 (2025): 2408883.
https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/pdf/10.1155/er/2408883 (ermo-MSDI,
acc 6-23-2025) KHAN ACRONYMS carbon emission (CEM) environmental
technology (EVT) economic policy uncertainty (EPU) renewable energy
transition (RET) environmental governance (EVG) Rapid urbanization
transition (URT)

Lastly, [[it is crucial to **tailor** policy recommendations to Arctic
nations' specific characteristics and contexts]{.underline}]{.mark},
including Canada, Denmark, Finland, Norway, Russia, Sweden, and the
United States. [[These]{.underline}]{.mark} countries have [[unique
economic, environmental, and social landscapes influencing their EVG and
climate action]{.underline}]{.mark} approach. For instance, [Canada and
the United States]{.underline}, with vast northern territories and
significant indigenous populations, [may focus on integrating
traditional ecological knowledge]{.underline} and prioritizing
community-driven sustainable development initiatives that respect
indigenous rights and livelihoods. [Denmark, through Greenland, has
distinct challenges]{.underline} related to its remote, small
communities, and could benefit from localized renewable energy solutions
like wind and hydropower, which align with its existing renewable energy
strengths. [Finland and Sweden]{.underline}, with advanced technology
sectors, [could lead in]{.underline} the [development of cutting-edge
environmental technologies and innovations]{.underline}, such as
advanced biofuels or smart grids, leveraging their strong research and
innovation capacities. With its significant investments in hydroelectric
power and a history of leadership in maritime technology, Norway could
prioritize enhancing cleaner maritime transport and expanding its
existing clean energy infrastructure. Given its vast natural resources
and heavy reliance on fossil fuels, [Russia may need to focus on
diversifying its energy mix]{.underline} and improving energy
efficiency, particularly in industrial sectors, while navigating complex
political and economic landscapes. Also, with its large and varied
Arctic region, [[the United States could **leverage** its technological
capabilities and economic resources to drive large-scale renewable
energy projects]{.underline}]{.mark} and robust EVG frameworks. By
considering these varied characteristics, the policy directions can be
more relevant and impactful, [[ensuring that each Arctic nation's
**unique** strengths and challenges are addressed in the collective
effort]{.underline}]{.mark} to improve environmental quality
[[**across** the Arctic region]{.underline}]{.mark}.

## Solvency - Turns

### 1NC \-- Settlement Turn

#### Plan facilitates open source RE housing in Greenland -- overcomes diesel dependence

Alyssa **Pantaleo**, 20**24**, **PhD, Engineering -- Dartmouth U**
\"Energy and Empowerment in the High Arctic\" (2024). Dartmouth College
Ph.D Dissertations. 281. NSF1927845 grant
<https://digitalcommons.dartmouth.edu/dissertations/281> (ermo-MSDI, acc
6-21-2025)

5.2 Future Work This research could be expanded on in several meaningful
ways and adapted to meet community needs around the Arctic or
communities in highly centralized governmental systems or remote areas.
The first potential future pathway is identifying and [[implementing
policy and financial tools for overcoming diesel
dependence]{.underline}]{.mark} in locations with high energy or fuel
subsidies and/or centralized utilities and governments. There is a
significant knowledge gap in the specific tools that [[would enable
community leaders to become]{.underline}]{.mark} meaningful
[[stakeholders]{.underline}]{.mark} in energy planning under these
conditions. There is also a gap in knowledge on how to lower energy
subsidies, [[incentivize renewable]{.underline}]{.mark} energy
([[and]{.underline}]{.mark} de-incentivize fossil fuel-based energy
consumption) while [[protect]{.underline}]{.mark}ing
[[vulnerable]{.underline}]{.mark} members of
[[communities]{.underline}]{.mark} who depend on energy subsidies. This
would require a thorough understanding of community and national energy
goals and developing place-based economic and energy frameworks. The
second pathway for expanding this research is developing [[the MILP
model]{.underline}]{.mark} into an [[open-sourced]{.underline}]{.mark}
platform that can be utilized by Nukissiorfiit or communities across
Greenland. This [[could allow]{.underline}]{.mark} communities across
[[Greenland]{.underline}]{.mark} to begin energy planning or finding
[[solar PV and BES combinations that]{.underline}]{.mark} would help
them meet their energy goals. The program could be simplified to allow
for open-sourced optimization solvers to handle fewer variables (for
example, reduce the time step from hourly to daily. Although this may
not present a realistic dispatch order for battery operation, it would
converge on a reasonable capacity addition to meet community goals.
Lastly, within this MILP program, stochastic load forecasting would
enhance the accuracy of the model and be a useful tool for Nukissiorfiit
in their research. Several research tools can [[effectively predict load
profiles for rural, isolated communities]{.underline}]{.mark} using
field data and surveys of residents \[149\].

#### Arctic urbanization drives ecological disruption

Mansoor **Khan**, 20**25**, 1 School of Intelligent Manufacturing and
Control **Engineering**, **Qilu Institute** of Technology, Jinan, China
ALSO - Hazrat Hassan ,2 Cai Li,3 Agyemang Kwasi Sampene ,3 and Francis
Kyere \"Achieving Carbon Neutrality Through Renewable Energy Transition
Amidst Economic Policy Uncertainty in the Arctic Region.\" International
Journal of Energy Research 2025.1 (2025): 2408883.
https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/pdf/10.1155/er/2408883 (ermo-MSDI,
acc 6-23-2025) KHAN ACRONYMS carbon emission (CEM) environmental
technology (EVT) economic policy uncertainty (EPU) renewable energy
transition (RET) environmental governance (EVG) Rapid urbanization
transition (URT)

[[URT was demonstrated to have a favorable and substantial influence on
CEM]{.underline}]{.mark} by 0.782% for the DSK method and 0.488 for
FMOLS [[in the Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark} countries. This finding is in
tandem with the theoretical proposition of this research by revealing
that [[as URT accelerates in the Arctic communities, driven
by]{.underline}]{.mark} indicators such as [[population growth and
economic expansion, infrastructure development, and energy use have
increased in tandem, raising CEM. The shift from a traditional lifestyle
to a more urbanized setting often involves greater dependence
on]{.underline}]{.mark} transportation, [[fossil
energy]{.underline}]{.mark}, and heating, [[which contribute
substantially to CEM]{.underline}]{.mark}. Among the Arctic nations,
issues such as land use, expanding industrial activities, and the demand
for modern amenities have intensified the carbon footprint in these
areas. Previous extant analysis aligns with a positive nexus between URT
and CEM. These studies argue that [[while URT brings an improved
standard of living, it often accompanies a surge in carbon-intensive
activities]{.underline}]{.mark} \[5, 79\]. Nevertheless, an analysis
presents conflicting evidence, suggesting that the URT can reduce CEM
\[80, 81\].

#### Arctic industrialization causes ecological disruption & resource conflicts

Ishfaq Hussain **Malik** 20**25** (University of **Leeds,**
Post-Doctoral Research Fellow at the School of **Geography**,) also
Rayees Ahmed (Divecha Centre for Climate Change, Indian Institute of
Science) James D. Ford (Priestley Centre for Climate Futures, University
of Leeds) Abdur Rahim Hamidi (Sustainability Research Institute, School
of Earth and Environment, University of Leeds) \"Arctic Warming:
Cascading Climate Impacts and Global Consequences.\" Climate 13.5
(2025): 85. https://www.mdpi.com/2225-1154/13/5/85 (ermo-MSDI, acc
6-23-2025)

While these changes have potential economic advantages, they also
represent major risks to the environment. [[Increased industrial
activity can cause pollution, habitat degradation, and social impacts,
which disproportionately affect Indigenous communities that rely on
Arctic ecosystem stability and resources]{.underline}]{.mark}
\[214,215\]. The possibility of [[transboundary conflicts over
resource]{.underline}]{.mark} claims [[emphasises the need for strong
governance frameworks]{.underline}]{.mark} that balance economic
development, environmental conservation, and community rights \[205\].
Despite these constraints, Arctic communities have demonstrated
extraordinary resilience through adaptive techniques and policy
initiatives. Indigenous knowledge systems, which emphasise sustainable
resource use and community-based monitoring, are crucial in responding
to climate change \[203,216\]. Co-management of wildlife resources, for
example, and the development of renewable energy initiatives minimise
reliance on foreign supply while also mitigating environmental harm
\[217\]. At the policy level, efforts, like the Arctic Council and
national adaptation programs, aim to boost resilience by encouraging
collaboration among local, national, and international stakeholders
\[212\]. These efforts underscore the need of combining scientific
research with Indigenous knowledge to provide equitable and effective
solutions \[218\] for the Arctic's rapidly changing climate.

### 1NC \-- Green Colonialism Turn

#### Fear of green colonialism hinders arctic climate transition

Marja Helena **Sivonen**, 20**25** **SYKE** Finnish Environment
Institute, Climate Solutions Unit - University of Tampere, Faculty of
Social Sciences, & Paula Kivimaa - SPRU, University of Sussex
\"Interrelations between security and the zero‑carbon energy transition
in the Finnish and Norwegian Arctic.\" Energy Research & Social Science
119 (2025): 103841.
<https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S2214629624004328>
(ermo-MSDI, acc 6-22-2025)

[[Other justifications for hindering the transition in the Arctic were
related to long distances, sparsely populated areas, disputes over land
use and claims of ownership]{.underline}]{.mark} and user rights among
Indigenous and non-Indigenous populations and the technological
challenges of cold climates. Previous research has also pointed out
[[the general populations´ concerns over "green grabbing" and
"Europeanization" in Norway, where]{.underline}]{.mark} legal and
physical [[control over renewable energy development is gradually
transferred to the EU, away from Norway]{.underline}]{.mark} \[76\].
Overall, land use questions are especially tightly bound up with
questions of human rights \[67\], an issue that was discussed in the
interviews but was absent in the strategy documents of both countries.
In Finland, the new Sámi Climate Council could be a fruitful body to
strengthen regime-niche interactions in a fair manner by allowing
non-state actors to have a role in decision-making, if under a mandate
of the state. It is yet to be seen what actual impact the council will
have over the medium and long term. Additionally, the role of the Sámi
Parliament could be enhanced in the decision-making.

The united global goal, to reduce greenhouse gas emissions and [[to

impacts Indigenous communities in the Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark}.
Currently, [[the Just Green Transition is dependent]{.underline}]{.mark}

takes place [[on Indigenous land]{.underline}]{.mark} in the name of the
"Green New Deal" (Robbins and Zografos, 2020). This has been [[termed
"Green Colonialism]{.underline}]{.mark}," the phrase was first used by
Aili Keskitalo, the former president of the Sámi Parliament in Norway,
to describe the impact of extractive industries and energy development
projects on Indigenous land (Normann, 2022, pg. 13; Saami-Council,
2017). Whilst [[there are undoubtedly detrimental
consequences]{.underline}]{.mark} of mining in the Arctic, with many of
these impacts being deemed [[acts of "Green
Colonialism]{.underline}]{.mark}" it is remiss to not acknowledge the

### 1NC \-- Security Turn

#### Reworking securitization enables non-linear disruptive transition to overcome path dependencies

Marja Helena **Sivonen**, 20**25** **SYKE** Finnish Environment
Institute, Climate Solutions Unit - University of Tampere, Faculty of
Social Sciences, & Paula Kivimaa - SPRU, University of Sussex
\"Interrelations between security and the zero‑carbon energy transition
in the Finnish and Norwegian Arctic.\" Energy Research & Social Science
119 (2025): 103841.
<https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S2214629624004328>
(ermo-MSDI, acc 6-22-2025)

[[Security is tied to Arctic energy transitions]{.underline}]{.mark} in
different ways. [[The area continues to be of geopolitical importance to
major global superpowers, including]{.underline}]{.mark} militarisation
by [[Russia]{.underline}]{.mark} \[6\] [[and]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[NATO]{.underline}]{.mark} \[7\]. Meanwhile, [[disputes over land use
rights]{.underline}]{.mark} at local and regional levels [[cause active
disruptions]{.underline}]{.mark} to Arctic residents\' lives \[8,9\],
posing more local effects on security. Hence, this paper takes the nexus
of zero‑carbon energy transitions and security in the Arctic as its
focus. It combines sustainability transitions research with sociology
and the concepts of positive and negative security to conduct analysis
of this nexus pertaining to Norwegian and Finnish Arctic areas.

[[Zero‑carbon transitions are "disruptive, contested and non-linear
processes]{.underline}]{.mark}" (\[10\], p. 464), [[which require
cultural and political changes that are difficult to
achieve]{.underline}]{.mark}. Thus, [[to capture the complexity of
transitions, a socio-technical lens is often used]{.underline}]{.mark}
\[10\]. Sustainability transitions research is essentially interested in
how socio-technical systems change to become more environmentally and
socially sustainable. It thus offers a fruitful avenue to examine Arctic
issues, [[as Arctic challenges related to sustainability are diverse in
scale, systems of governance, and the actors
involved]{.underline}]{.mark} \[11,12\]. In terms of sustainable
production and consumption, [[several challenges are regarded as
significant to lock-ins: strong path dependencies, institutional and
cultural obstacles and obstructing practices \[13\]. In the Arctic,
these are all **amplified**]{.underline}]{.mark} with extreme weather
conditions, global interests and local conditions enabling a good life.

A frequently used framework to study sustainability transitions is [[the
multi-level perspective]{.underline}]{.mark} (MLP) which addresses
changes [[in socio-technical systems]{.underline}]{.mark} \[10\]. Such
systems are formed and [[become relatively stable over
time]{.underline}]{.mark}, so the complementary elements of
technologies, infrastructure, rules and practices resist change due to
path dependencies.

Drawing from security studies, we use the [[concepts of positive and
negative security]{.underline}]{.mark} to [[understand the
dynamics]{.underline}]{.mark} more broadly during the socio-technical
system change. Originally introduced to broaden from state- and
threat-centred security thinking (i.e. negative security), [[positive
security]{.underline}]{.mark} emphasises the importance of human
perception in international relations theorisation \[14\] and [[offers
an alternative]{.underline}]{.mark} to the traditional negative security
-oriented approach \[15\]. Positive security has been used to refer to
conditions that improve human wellbeing and promote peace by enabling
communities and individuals \[16,17\].

Previous research has shown that, although national and international
policy-making encourages cleaner energy production and consumption,
[[inertia perpetuates the resource extraction
industry]{.underline}]{.mark} in the Eurasian Arctic \[2,18\]. In this
paper, we investigate two national perspectives on the Arctic,
comprising Lapland in Finland and the "High North" in Norway. Norway is
a major oil and gas exporter with a long coastline and keen interests
also in fisheries, on-shore energy production, security and cooperation
between other Arctic states. Unlike Norway, Finland has no coastline nor
oil and gas in the Arctic but increased interests in military and
security cooperation within NATO member states and significant mineral
deposits and wind energy potential. However, both countries\' plans are
not without disputes regarding land use conflicts, growing needs of the
tourism sector and the responsibilities to ensure the indigenous
people\'s traditional way of life. The two countries are officially
committed to climate change mitigation via the Paris Agreement. Finland
is a European Union (EU) member state, while Norway complies with some
EU legislation via its signature to the European Economic Area (EEA)
agreement.

Based on previous research, our hypothesis is that issues regarded as
negative security are used to justify military presence in the Arctic
and continued fossil fuel extraction, thus prompting a hindered
transition. Further, the paper aims to answer two questions: How are
energy transitions and security intertwined in the Finnish and Norwegian
Arctic, and what are the justifications for hindered transitions?

As sustainability transitions are deeply political processes \[19\],
this study contributes to understanding political discourse, called for
in the transitions literature \[20\]. To capture the processes of
[[sustainability transitions in the Norwegian and Finnish
Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark}, we [[concentrate on niche- and regime-level
interaction]{.underline}]{.mark} and pay attention to the actors that
influence and navigate sustainability transitions \[21\].

#### Security evolution is complicated & difficult -- disrupts arctic climate transitions

Marja Helena **Sivonen**, 20**25** **SYKE** Finnish Environment
Institute, Climate Solutions Unit - University of Tampere, Faculty of
Social Sciences, & Paula Kivimaa - SPRU, University of Sussex
\"Interrelations between security and the zero‑carbon energy transition
in the Finnish and Norwegian Arctic.\" Energy Research & Social Science
119 (2025): 103841.
<https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S2214629624004328>
(ermo-MSDI, acc 6-22-2025)

In our strategy document analysis, we did not find many mentions of
regime decline in either Finland or Norway. In fact, we created a new
code for the support the regime received in those documents. [[In
Norway, support for fossil fuel production correlates well with national
ambitions and policies]{.underline}]{.mark} \[79\]. However in Finland,
phasing out fossil fuels is an important part of national climate and
energy strategies \[80\]. The support that [[Arctic strategies appeared
to show for continued fossil fuel production or use in both
countries]{.underline}]{.mark} connects to positive and negative notions
of security. [[In negative security thinking]{.underline}]{.mark},
military and national interests, sovereignty and landownership are
central. This is present when [[Norway claims sea areas with oil and gas
industry installations and]{.underline}]{.mark} when [[economic
advancements in Finland are sought from cold-climate
technology]{.underline}]{.mark} development,
[[enabling]{.underline}]{.mark}, for instance, [[winter passage and
machine durability]{.underline}]{.mark}. The connection to positive
security can be found in both countries\' aims to keep remote areas
inhabited by enabling safe socio-economic wellbeing that will encourage
people to stay in the area. Infrastructure and other state-provided
basic living condition elements are needed to keep people in the north,
along with cultural, political and technical solutions that can offer
the same living standards as in the south. Here, positive security as a
multi-actor discursive practice \[15\] provides a useful perspective;
the Norwegian strategies claim support of and possibilities for local
and Indigenous populations to take part in decision-making, but our
analysis echoes previous research that more is needed \[9,81\]. This is
significant because [[democratising security thinking can foster change
in practice. However, it is by no means an easy process, from
ontological differences]{.underline}]{.mark} \[8\] [[through land use
and]{.underline}]{.mark} the costs and benefits of, for instance,
[[mining activities]{.underline}]{.mark} \[82\]. [[The latter may either
contrast energy transitions with security]{.underline}]{.mark} (when
energy-related land use threatens reindeer herding or broader
environmental security) [[or align them]{.underline}]{.mark} (when
mining creates employment in the north and supports the energy
transition), [[showing the complexities of the energy-security
nexus]{.underline}]{.mark}.

### 1NC \-- Arctic Paradox Turn 

#### Arctic RE leads to cultural disruption

Anna K **Bidgood**, 20**24** (Earth and Planets Laboratory, **Carnegie**
Institution for Science) & Jessica Hall (Centre for Sámi Studies, UiT
The Arctic University of Norway) \"We Need to Talk About Mining in the
Arctic.\" Earth Science, Systems and Society 4.1 (2024): 10117.
<https://www.lyellcollection.org/doi/pdf/10.3389/esss.2024.10117>
(ermo-MSDI, acc 6-23-2025)

The [[Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark} is experiencing the impact of climate
change much faster than other parts of the world. Whilst warming
climates expand access to navigation opportunities and untapped mineral
resources, they at the same time bring lasting impacts on the wellbeing
of its people and the environment. Additionally, [[resource extraction,
including raw materials for **low carbon** technology, has the potential
to **disrupt** the social, cultural, and ecological wellbeing in
project-affected areas]{.underline}]{.mark} (Larsen and Ingimundarson,
2023). [[This is the **Arctic paradox**, where our need for solutions to
combat climate change have the potential to have negative impacts on the
people and environments most affected]{.underline}]{.mark}.

#### Arctic indigenous knowledge critical to climate adaptation

Ishfaq Hussain **Malik** 20**25** (University of **Leeds,**
Post-Doctoral Research Fellow at the School of **Geography**,) also
Rayees Ahmed (Divecha Centre for Climate Change, Indian Institute of

6-23-2025)

The collected literature was methodically organised into [[major themes,
such as Arctic amplification, sea ice dynamics, permafrost thaw,
ecosystem alterations, socioeconomic implications, and policy
responses]{.underline}]{.mark}. A thematic content analysis was carried
out to analyse recurring trends, causal relationships, and emerging
challenges across these themes. This method focused [[on feedback
mechanisms, such as ice--albedo interactions and permafrost--carbon
dynamics, as well as their consequences for global
climate]{.underline}]{.mark} systems. Second, the study critically
synthesised [[emerging patterns]{.underline}]{.mark}, linking findings
to regional and global contexts. Visualisation tools, [[such
as]{.underline}]{.mark} geographic information systems (GISs) and
data-driven infographics, were used to show spatial and temporal
[[trends in Arctic warming, sea ice loss]{.underline}]{.mark}, and
ecosystem impact. Figures were created to represent various phenomena,
such as [[Arctic amplification and cascading ecosystem
impacts]{.underline}]{.mark}, assuring clarity and accessibility.
Finally, knowledge gaps and future research goals were identified using
a gap analysis approach. This includes [[comparing]{.underline}]{.mark}
current findings [[to existing policy and governance
frameworks]{.underline}]{.mark}, emphasising the importance of
interdisciplinary and participatory approaches. [[The incorporation of
Indigenous ecological knowledge and scientific approaches was identified
as an important pathway for developing long-term adaption
strategies]{.underline}]{.mark}.

#### Local indigenous disruption DA

To answer the first research question of how renewable energy and
security are intertwined, we can say that they were connected to each
other on various ways. In both countries, [[land use disputes over wind
production, minerals mining and expanding electricity transmission
networks]{.underline}]{.mark} were directly discussed as positive
security issues, as "the everyday" is intricate to those developments.
They [[imply significant changes to the inhabitants,
and]{.underline}]{.mark} for the Sámi also [[their right to their
traditional culture and way of life]{.underline}]{.mark}. Security was
defined to be of multi-actor premises, where ability to participate in
decision-making and defining what security is and to whom it should be
provided when energy transitions proceed was highlighted. As our
analysis has revealed, notions of justice are deeply an aspect of
security. Indeed, many interviewees wished to discuss issues of
injustice in the context of security, even when not directly asked about
them.

belts, Proterozoic to Mesozoic large igneous provinces and large
sedimentary basins of multiple ages (Harrison and St-Onge, 2023; Figure
1). The Arctic is also host to a range of [[critical metals such as Ni,
Zn, REEs and Cu]{.underline}]{.mark} which [[are essential for renewable
technologies, such as wind]{.underline}]{.mark} turbines [[and
solar]{.underline}]{.mark} panels (Boyd et al., 2016). [[There are
numerous world-class deposits]{.underline}]{.mark} found [[in the
Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark} which have produced significant amounts of
raw materials [[including]{.underline}]{.mark} Red Dog Zn-Pb mine in
northern [[Alaska and]{.underline}]{.mark} Norilsk Ni-Pd mine in
[[Russia]{.underline}]{.mark}. The Kiruna Mine, located in northern
[[Sweden]{.underline}]{.mark}, is one of the world's largest underground
iron-ore mines. Similarly, Raglan mine in the Nunavik region of
[[Quebec]{.underline}]{.mark} is a large nickel mining complex dating
back to 1931. Despite these known occurrences of minerals, [[large
parts]{.underline}]{.mark} of the Arctic [[are]{.underline}]{.mark}
relatively [[underexplored, introducing significant potential for
additional resources to be discovered]{.underline}]{.mark}.

#### Turn is UQ -- blended food

Adrian **Unc**, 20**25**, Memorial University of Newfoundland, School of

Blended Arctic food systems

[[There are growing movements in the Indigenous
North]{.underline}]{.mark}, often within communities,
[[that]{.underline}]{.mark} aim to both [[preserve traditional food
practices and integrate beneficial]{.underline}]{.mark} aspects of
[[modern food]{.underline}]{.mark} systems. While variably and
increasingly heterogeneous, current Arctic food systems must, at the
same time, target food sovereignty and address changing conditions, such
as economic growth, rapid, climate-driven ecosystem changes, and food
security. [[This can be accomplished by embracing locally adaptable
innovations that optimize traditional food systems and increase local
capacity]{.underline}]{.mark}for producing food that both meets local
demand and satisfies markets for imported foods. People in the Arctic
regions need food that utilizes local fuels for heat and production and
is primarily cultivated, harvested, prepared, preserved, shared, sold,

while allowing for additional ways to produce foods for export to
regional and global markets.

#### Indigenous input in arctic decisions rising now

Ishfaq Hussain **Malik** 20**25** (University of **Leeds,**
Post-Doctoral Research Fellow at the School of **Geography**,) also
Rayees Ahmed (Divecha Centre for Climate Change, Indian Institute of
Science) James D. Ford (Priestley Centre for Climate Futures, University
of Leeds) Abdur Rahim Hamidi (Sustainability Research Institute, School
of Earth and Environment, University of Leeds) \"Arctic Warming:
Cascading Climate Impacts and Global Consequences.\" Climate 13.5
(2025): 85. https://www.mdpi.com/2225-1154/13/5/85 (ermo-MSDI, acc
6-23-2025)

National policies in Arctic nations vary significantly, reflecting
different priorities in balancing economic development and environmental
conservation \[250\]. Canada's Arctic and Northern Policy Framework
prioritises sustainable development, renewable energy transitions, and
climate resilience, while also incorporating Indigenous knowledge into
government policies to some extent \[251\]. In contrast, Russia's Arctic
strategy focusses on resource extraction, particularly oil, gas, and
minerals, and prioritises economic gains over environmental preservation
\[252\]. The Nordic countries have adopted ambitious green policies,
such as transitioning to renewable energy and promoting marine protected
areas, but there are still challenges in reducing emissions from the
expanding maritime and tourism industries \[253\]. [[Indigenous
communities]{.underline}]{.mark}, important to the Arctic's social and
biological systems, [[are becoming more active in
policymaking]{.underline}]{.mark} \[50\]. [[Co-management initiatives
demonstrate the importance of combining traditional ecological knowledge
and scientific research]{.underline}]{.mark} to develop culturally
sensitive adaptation strategies \[254\]. Indigenous knowledge has proven
[[especially]{.underline}]{.mark} useful [[in monitoring climate
impacts]{.underline}]{.mark}, such as changes in species migration and
permafrost degradation \[255\]. However, the United Nations Declaration
on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples ([[UNDRIP]{.underline}]{.mark})
[[emphasises]{.underline}]{.mark} the importance of increased
[[political and financial empowerment]{.underline}]{.mark} in order to
promote fair participation in decision making \[256\]. [[Indigenous
organisations advocate for greater influence in shaping regional and
national policies]{.underline}]{.mark}, particularly those governing
land use, resource extraction, and conservation \[40,257,258\].

### 2NC \-- Arctic Paradox -- Turns Env Impacts 

#### Adaptation aff or CP

Ishfaq Hussain **Malik** 20**25** (University of **Leeds,**
Post-Doctoral Research Fellow at the School of **Geography**,) also
Rayees Ahmed (Divecha Centre for Climate Change, Indian Institute of
Science) James D. Ford (Priestley Centre for Climate Futures, University
of Leeds) Abdur Rahim Hamidi (Sustainability Research Institute, School
of Earth and Environment, University of Leeds) \"Arctic Warming:
Cascading Climate Impacts and Global Consequences.\" Climate 13.5
(2025): 85. https://www.mdpi.com/2225-1154/13/5/85 (ermo-MSDI, acc
6-23-2025)

Indigenous People are among the most susceptible to climate change, with
traditional subsistence activities disrupted by changes in wildlife
migration, sea ice loss, and permafrost thaw \[22,32\]. [[Adaptation
measures that incorporate Indigenous knowledge, like community-based
monitoring systems, have been beneficial in increasing
resilience]{.underline}]{.mark} \[51,273\]. For example,
[[collaborating]{.underline}]{.mark} on research with Indigenous
stakeholders has [[resulted in a better knowledge and management of
ecological changes]{.underline}]{.mark} \[30,31,32,274\]. Thawing
permafrost and coastal erosion pose significant dangers to Arctic
infrastructure, such as buildings, pipelines, and transportation
networks \[19,275\]. [[Engineering]{.underline}]{.mark} solutions, such
as permafrost-resistant materials and coastal defence systems, are being
deployed, but their [[high cost and]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[distant
locations of Arctic communities prevent widespread
adoption]{.underline}]{.mark} \[276,277,278\]. Policies that prioritise
climate-resilient infrastructure are important for mitigating these
risks \[152,279,280\].

Ecosystem-based adaptation strategies prioritise biodiversity
conservation and habitat restoration in order to preserve ecosystem
services that benefit communities \[281\]. Arctic wetlands, for example,
play an important role in carbon sequestration and water control; hence,
their preservation is a priority \[22\]. Protecting vital habitats for
migratory species has become an important adaptation strategy \[23\].
Integrating climate adaptation into national and regional strategies is
critical for good governance. [[Participatory scenario
planning]{.underline}]{.mark}, which combines multiple stakeholder
viewpoints, including those of Indigenous communities, [[has proven
effective in developing adaptive management
strategies]{.underline}]{.mark} \[282\]. [[However]{.underline}]{.mark},
research shows that [[it frequently fails to fully integrate with
Indigenous practices]{.underline}]{.mark}, emphasising the need for more
inclusive approaches \[282\]. Cooperative agreements, such as the Arctic
Fisheries Agreement, illustrate the possibility for multilateral
governance, but gaps exist in addressing the security implications of
rising human activity in the region \[36,50\]. [[Strengthening
international agreements on Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark} shipping,
fishing, and [[resource management is important for
balancing]{.underline}]{.mark} economic [[development
and]{.underline}]{.mark} environmental
[[sustainability]{.underline}]{.mark}.

#### Centering northern voices is critical for sustainability

Anna K **Bidgood**, 20**24** (Earth and Planets Laboratory, **Carnegie**
Institution for Science) & Jessica Hall (Centre for Sámi Studies, UiT
The Arctic University of Norway) \"We Need to Talk About Mining in the
Arctic.\" Earth Science, Systems and Society 4.1 (2024): 10117.
<https://www.lyellcollection.org/doi/pdf/10.3389/esss.2024.10117>
(ermo-MSDI, acc 6-23-2025)

The snow- and ice-covered mountains of Lofoten in Norway provided a
truly dramatic and breathtaking backdrop to the workshop on the "Green
Shift" in the Arctic, part of the 2023 Arctic Frontiers conference. Sat
here amidst the local communities, the fisheries and the stunning
mountain scenery, the two authors of this paper met, alongside 32 young
and emerging leaders from different walks of life and industries across
the Arctic. The combined knowledge of our group covered a wide range of
perspectives and [[personal experiences gained through living in the
Arctic, Indigenous knowledge]{.underline}]{.mark}, and knowledge
[[specific to]{.underline}]{.mark} an
[[Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark}-[[based industry]{.underline}]{.mark}
including fisheries, renewables and many more. In the workshop we found
that our diverse group [[share]{.underline}]{.mark}d [[a common vision
for the Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark}, one of creating a sustainable
north, [[where voices and decisions]{.underline}]{.mark} of the North
are [[from the North]{.underline}]{.mark} and planetary and human
welfare [[are at the forefront]{.underline}]{.mark} of our actions.

[[Mining is a major industry in the Arctic with huge potential to

#### Plan violates UNDRIP

Anna K **Bidgood**, 20**24** (Earth and Planets Laboratory, **Carnegie**
Institution for Science) & Jessica Hall (Centre for Sámi Studies, UiT
The Arctic University of Norway) \"We Need to Talk About Mining in the
Arctic.\" Earth Science, Systems and Society 4.1 (2024): 10117.
<https://www.lyellcollection.org/doi/pdf/10.3389/esss.2024.10117>
(ermo-MSDI, acc 6-23-2025)

One example of this is The United Nations Declaration on the Rights of
Indigenous Peoples ([[UNDRIP]{.underline}]{.mark}) which was adopted in
2007 and [[establishes a **universal** framework of minimum standards
for the survival]{.underline}]{.mark}, dignity and wellbeing [[of
Indigenous Peoples worldwide]{.underline}]{.mark} (United Nations,
2015). [[UNDRIP stipulates]{.underline}]{.mark} that [[Indigenous
Peoples are not solely stakeholders]{.underline}]{.mark} in potential
extractive projects [[but **instead**]{.underline}]{.mark}, they are
[[right holders]{.underline}]{.mark}. The [[ratification of UNDRIP led
to **change** throughout the Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark}. For example,
in Greenland, the 30 year old "Greenland [[Home
Rule]{.underline}]{.mark} Arrangement" [[was
replaced]{.underline}]{.mark} in 2009 [[by]{.underline}]{.mark} the Act
on Greenland [[Self Government]{.underline}]{.mark}. This provided the
legal basis for Greenland gaining authority, previously held by Denmark,
in matters exclusively related to Greenlandic domestic affairs and
natural resources (International Energy Agency, 2023). Furthermore, in
2011, the Government of Sweden [[added]{.underline}]{.mark} a reform
[[to the Constitution]{.underline}]{.mark}, stipulating that the
Government shall encourage opportunities for the Sámi people and for
other ethnic, linguistic and religious minorities in Sweden to preserve
and develop their own culture and community life (Human Rights Council
Report, 2014).

[[An integral part of UNDRIP is]{.underline}]{.mark} the notion of
[[Free, Prior and Informed Consent]{.underline}]{.mark} (FPIC) which
stipulates that states must have obtained free, prior and informed
consent [[**before** adopting and implementing]{.underline}]{.mark}
legislative or administrative [[measures which impact Indigenous
Peoples]{.underline}]{.mark}. The recognition and promotion of the Free
Prior Informed [[Consent is an important principle]{.underline}]{.mark}
of engaging [[with Indigenous peoples during mining]{.underline}]{.mark}
projects and has become very important [[worldwide]{.underline}]{.mark}.
The International Finance Corporation, a member of [[the World
Bank]{.underline}]{.mark}, has [[created eight performance
standards]{.underline}]{.mark} which aim to provide guidance on how to
avoid, mitigate and manage risks [[so]{.underline}]{.mark} that
[[business is]{.underline}]{.mark} conducted in a
[[sustainable]{.underline}]{.mark} way (International Finance
Corporation, 2012a). These standards recognise that Indigenous Peoples
may be particularly vulnerable to the adverse impacts associated with
project development, including the loss of identity, culture, and
natural resource-based livelihoods (International Finance Corporation,
2012b). The implementation of FPIC has also been seen in the mining
industry. In 2013, the International Council on Mining and Metals
([[ICMM]{.underline}]{.mark}) published a position statement on the
rights of Indigenous peoples [[incorporating FPIC
principles]{.underline}]{.mark} and promoting constructive relationships
between mining companies and Indigenous peoples. ICMM [[noted the
increase]{.underline}]{.mark} in acknowledgement and application of FPIC
in the mining industry [[since]{.underline}]{.mark} the Mining, Minerals
and Sustainable Development (MMSD) initiative was completed, [[12 years
ago]{.underline}]{.mark}.

In addition to the protections provided by UNDRIP and FPIC, the
International Labour Organisation Convention formed the Indigenous and
Tribals Peoples Convention in 1989, which is often referred to as [[ILO
No. 169, recognising Indigenous Peoples' right to
self-determination]{.underline}]{.mark} and paving the way for the
creation and implementation of UNDRIP. A previous ILO convention, No.
107 formed in 1957, was created in the wake of WW2 amidst rising concern
regarding the oppression and discrimination of Indigenous Peoples.
[[Norway]{.underline}]{.mark} declined to sign this convention in 1958,
noting that the provisions included did not apply to the Sámi (Ravna,
2020) but [[became the first to ratify]{.underline}]{.mark} ILO No. 169
in 1989. As one of the signatory states, Norway is obliged [[to
recognise Sámi self determination]{.underline}]{.mark} and within this
is the restitution of Sámi culture and heritage. An example of this is
the Bååstede project which has seen around 1,600 Sámi cultural heritage
artefacts from the Norwegian Folk Museum and the Museum of Cultural
History in Oslo, being returned to six Sámi museum siidas. Furthermore,
the National Archives of Norway handed over the Lapp Codicil which is an
addendum to the Stromstad Treaty of 1751 which defined the
Norwegian-Swedish border, to the Sámi Archives, located in Kautokeino in
2021. This addendum to the border treaty contains provisions on the
right of reindeer-herding between the Swedish and Norwegian border (The
Library of Congress, 2021).

#### 10% of arctic residents are indigenous -- consulting them on extraction critical to justice

Anna K **Bidgood**, 20**24** (Earth and Planets Laboratory, **Carnegie**
Institution for Science) & Jessica Hall (Centre for Sámi Studies, UiT
The Arctic University of Norway) \"We Need to Talk About Mining in the
Arctic.\" Earth Science, Systems and Society 4.1 (2024): 10117.
<https://www.lyellcollection.org/doi/pdf/10.3389/esss.2024.10117>
(ermo-MSDI, acc 6-23-2025)

The [[Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark} encompasses a myriad of ecosystems,
transcending borders between nations and cultures. It is home to 4
million people, including numerous [[Indigenous groups making up around
10% of the population]{.underline}]{.mark}. Although underexplored, it
is also host to a variety of geologically diverse mineral deposits that
[[are critical]{.underline}]{.mark} to the production of renewable
energy and our ability to achieve our climate goals. However, the impact
of climate change on the Arctic is magnified and some of our solutions
to climate change have the potential to have negative local impacts.
Furthermore, the history of mining in the Arctic raises understandable
concerns as to whether or not we should be exploring and mining in the
Arctic. This article discusses the interplay between the environment,
people and development in the Arctic, with a specific focus on the
history of exploration and mining in the region. We pose [[questions
such as]{.underline}]{.mark}: "How do we [[balance]{.underline}]{.mark}
the global need for minerals with [[environmental and social concerns
around resource extraction]{.underline}]{.mark}?", and "can we envisage
a future for mining in the Arctic which ensures long-term
sustainability, environmental stewardship and Indigenous wellbeing and
collaboration?" [[The answer]{.underline}]{.mark} to some of these
questions [[might lie in]{.underline}]{.mark} examples of more
successful resource development in the Arctic, which include
[[Indigenous benefit agreements, braided knowledge systems and shared
ownership projects]{.underline}]{.mark}. It is clear that [[only by
incorporating a diversity of voices and partnerships, and challenging
business as usual in the Arctic, can we]{.underline}]{.mark} begin to
[[conceive]{.underline}]{.mark} of potential solutions for achieving [[a
just transition]{.underline}]{.mark}.

### 1NC \-- Arctic is Resilient

#### Arctic resilience mitigates the impact

Ishfaq Hussain **Malik** 20**25** (University of **Leeds,**
Post-Doctoral Research Fellow at the School of **Geography**,) also

While these changes have potential economic advantages, they also
represent major risks to the environment. Increased industrial activity
can cause pollution, habitat degradation, and social impacts, which
disproportionately affect Indigenous communities that rely on Arctic
ecosystem stability and resources \[214,215\]. The possibility of
[[transboundary conflicts over resource claims emphasises the need for
strong governance frameworks]{.underline}]{.mark} that balance economic
development, environmental conservation, and community rights \[205\].
Despite these constraints, [[Arctic communities have demonstrated
extraordinary resilience through adaptive techniques and policy
initiatives. Indigenous knowledge systems]{.underline}]{.mark}, which
emphasise sustainable resource use and community-based monitoring, are
crucial in [[respond]{.underline}]{.mark}ing [[to climate
change]{.underline}]{.mark} \[203,216\]. [[Co-management of wildlife
resources]{.underline}]{.mark}, for example, and the development of

[[policy level, efforts, like the Arctic Council and national adaptation
programs, aim to boost resilience]{.underline}]{.mark} by encouraging
collaboration among local, national, and international stakeholders
\[212\]. These efforts underscore the need of combining scientific
research with Indigenous knowledge to provide equitable and effective
solutions \[218\] for the Arctic's rapidly changing climate.

#### Wet arctic good -- sinks the GHG from thaw

### 1NC \-- AT: Amplification

#### Amplification poorly understood -- may be overstated -- no solvency

Ishfaq Hussain **Malik** 20**25** (University of **Leeds,**
Post-Doctoral Research Fellow at the School of **Geography**,) also
Rayees Ahmed (Divecha Centre for Climate Change, Indian Institute of
Science) James D. Ford (Priestley Centre for Climate Futures, University
of Leeds) Abdur Rahim Hamidi (Sustainability Research Institute, School
of Earth and Environment, University of Leeds) \"Arctic Warming:
Cascading Climate Impacts and Global Consequences.\" Climate 13.5
(2025): 85. https://www.mdpi.com/2225-1154/13/5/85 (ermo-MSDI, acc
6-23-2025)

[[Arctic amplification]{.underline}]{.mark} is a process in which the
Arctic region warms at a much higher rate than the global average, as
supported by instrumental evidence, climate models, and paleoclimate
proxy records \[135,136\]. Several connected mechanisms contribute to
this rapid warming, including marine heating and the ice--albedo
feedback mechanism (Figure 3), which exposes darker ocean surfaces when
sea ice melts, resulting in higher solar energy absorption \[4\].
Atmospheric feedback mechanisms, such as Planck feedback, lapse-rate
feedback, and cloud feedback, as well as ocean heat transport and
meridional atmospheric moisture transport, also play important roles
\[137,138\]. Recent decreases in air pollution in Europe \[139\] have
been related to increasing Arctic warming, and future reductions in
Asian aerosols owing to mitigation policies may exacerbate this trend
\[140\]. [[Despite]{.underline}]{.mark} the well-known [[trends, there
is little agreement on the exact magnitude]{.underline}]{.mark} of AA,
with studies reporting Arctic warming at nearly twice, thrice, and four
times the global average. The Arctic Monitoring and Assessment Programme
reports a rate of warming three times faster than global warming from
1971 to 2019 \[141\]. The [[differences stem from different definitions
of AA]{.underline}]{.mark}, such as the time periods and geographical
limits examined. While [[climate models]{.underline}]{.mark}, notably
those in the Coupled Model Intercomparison Project phase 6 (CMIP6), have
made progress in modelling Arctic climate evolution and sea ice
dynamics, many continue to [[struggle to
appropriately]{.underline}]{.mark} depict the
[[sensitivity]{.underline}]{.mark} of sea ice loss to global temperature
increases. Understanding whether climate models can effectively
reproduce the reported scale of AA is important, given that sea ice loss
is an important cause of the phenomena. Although previous research
suggests that AA is weaker in climate models than in observations
\[142\], [[a complete comparison with]{.underline}]{.mark} the most
[[recent]{.underline}]{.mark} observational [[datasets has yet to be
undertaken]{.underline}]{.mark}. Arctic amplification is an important
climate phenomenon with far-reaching implications for global climate
systems, [[emphasising the need for further research and better climate
models to support effective policy]{.underline}]{.mark} decisions
targeted at minimising climate change impacts.

AA is a well-known phenomenon characterised by increased warming in the
Arctic relative to the global average, primarily caused by such
processes as sea ice--albedo feedback, which reduces the surface albedo
as ice melts, exposing more ocean that absorbs heat \[143,144\]. Recent
research has highlighted the subtle functions of cloud and water vapour
feedback mechanisms, with increased cloud cover blocking incoming solar
radiation while increasing downward longwave radiation, resulting in net
warming \[145\]. Temperature feedback, influenced by stable
stratification of the Arctic atmosphere, leads to positive feedback via
a lower lapse rate, resulting in higher warmth aloft \[146\]. The
movement of heat and water vapour from lower latitudes to the Arctic
exacerbates regional warming \[10,147\]. [[Despite]{.underline}]{.mark}
a thorough [[understanding]{.underline}]{.mark} of these [[mechanisms,
quantifying their relative contributions is difficult,
implying]{.underline}]{.mark} that [[Arctic amplification persists even
when]{.underline}]{.mark} specific feedback mechanisms are
[[suppressed]{.underline}]{.mark}, resulting in dynamic competition
among feedback processes in climate models \[142\].

### 1NC \-- Warming Defense

#### Warming won't be catastrophic

**Zycher 21** -- Dr. Benjamin, Senior Fellow at the American Enterprise
Institute, Doctorate in Economics from UCLA, Master in Public Policy
from the University of California, Berkeley, and Bachelor of Arts in
Political Science from UCLA, Former Senior Economist at the RAND
Corporation, Former Adjunct Professor of Economics at the University of
California, Los Angeles (UCLA) and at the California State University
Channel Islands, and Former Senior Economist at the Jet Propulsion
Laboratory, California Institute of Technology. "The Case for Climate
Change Realism", AEI,
<https://www.aei.org/articles/the-case-for-climate-change-realism/>,
06-21-2021

Unable to demonstrate that observed climate trends are due to
anthropogenic climate change --- or even that these events are
particularly unusual or concerning --- **[climate
catastrophists]{.underline}** will often **[turn to [dire
predictions]{.mark}]{.underline}** about prospective climate phenomena.
**[The problem]{.underline}** with such predictions **[is]{.underline}**
that **[they [are]{.mark} almost always [generated by]{.mark} climate
[models driven by highly complex]{.mark} sets of [assumptions]{.mark}
about which there is significant dispute. Worse, these models are
[notorious for failing]{.mark} to accurately predict already documented
changes in climate]{.underline}**. As climatologist Patrick Michaels of
the Competitive Enterprise Institute notes:

**[[During all periods]{.underline}]{.mark}** from 10 years (2006-2015)
to 65 (1951-2015) years in length, **[the [observed temp]{.mark}erature
trend [lies in the]{.mark} lower half of]{.underline}** the collection
of **[climate model simulations, and for several periods it lies very
close]{.underline}** (or even below) **[the [2.5th percentile]{.mark} of
all the model runs]{.underline}**. Over shorter periods, such as the
last two decades, a plethora of mechanisms have been put forth to
explain the observed/modeled divergence, but none do so completely and
many of the explanations are inconsistent with each other.

Similarly, climatologist John Christy of the University of Alabama in
Huntsville observes that **[almost [all]{.mark} of the 102 climate
[models]{.mark}]{.underline}** incorporated into the Coupled Model
Intercomparison Project (CMIP) --- a tracking effort conducted by the
Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory --- **[[overstate]{.mark} past
and current temperature [trends by a factor of]{.mark} two to
[three]{.mark}, and at times even more]{.underline}**. It seems
axiomatic to say **[we should not rely on climate models]{.underline}**
that are unable to predict the past or the present to make predictions
about the distant future.

The overall temperature trend is not the only parameter the models
predict poorly. As an example, **[every]{.underline}** CMIP **[climate
model predicts that increases in atmospheric concentrations of
greenhouse gas should create an enhanced heating effect
in]{.underline}** the mid-troposphere **[over the
tropics]{.underline}** --- that is, at an altitude over the tropics of
about 30,000-40,000 feet. The underlying climatology is simple: Most of
the tropics is ocean, and as increases in greenhouse-gas concentrations
warm the Earth slightly, there should be an increase in the evaporation
of ocean water in this region. When the water vapor rises into the
mid-troposphere, it condenses, releasing heat. And **[yet]{.underline}**
the **[satellites cannot find this heating effect --- a reality
suggesting that our understanding of climate and atmospheric phenomena
is not as robust as many seem to assume]{.underline}**.

**[The [poor]{.mark} predictive [record]{.mark} of mainstream climate
models [is exacerbated by]{.mark} the [tendency]{.mark} of the IPCC and
U.S. government agencies [to assume]{.mark} highly [unrealistic]{.mark}
future [increases]{.mark} in greenhouse-gas
concentrations]{.underline}**. The IPCC's 2014 Fifth Assessment Report,
for example, uses four alternative "representative concentration
pathways" to outline scenarios of increased greenhouse-gas
concentrations yielding anthropogenic warming. These scenarios are known
as RCP2.6, RCP4.5, RCP6, and RCP8.5. Since 1950, the average annual
increase in greenhouse-gas concentrations has been about 1.6 parts per
million. The average annual increase from 1985 to 2019 was about 1.9
parts per million, and from 2000 to 2019, it was about 2.2 parts per
million. The largest increase that occurred was about 3.4 parts per
million in 2016. But the assumed average annual increases in
greenhouse-gas concentrations through 2100 under the four RCPs are 1.1,
3.0, 5.5, and an astounding 11.9 parts per million, respectively.

The studies generating the most alarmist predictions are the IPCC's
Special Report on Global Warming of 1.5°C and the U.S. government's
Fourth National Climate Assessment, both of which were published in
2018. Both assume RCP8.5 as the scenario most relevant for policy
planning. **[The [average]{.mark}]{.underline}** annual
**[g]{.underline}**reen**[h]{.underline}**ouse-**[g]{.underline}**as
**[[increase]{.underline}]{.mark}** under RCP8.5 **[[is]{.mark} over
[five times]{.mark} the [annual]{.mark} average]{.underline}** for
2000-2019 **[and almost four times the single biggest increase on
record]{.underline}**. Climatologist Judith Curry, formerly of the
Georgia Institute of Technology, describes such a scenario as
**[["borderline impossible."]{.underline}]{.mark}**

### 2NC \-- Warming Defense

#### Nuke war [outweighs]{.underline} climate change. 

Bjorn **Lomborg 22**. Former director of the Danish
government\'s [Environmental Assessment

[These and other issues plaguing the developing world are solvable, but

change]{.underline}. Giving the developing world affordable access to
consistently available energy---which often requires fossil fuels---is
the key to lifting most of the world out of poverty. Yet before the

energy more expensive and less accessible for the world's poorest. [What
