### 1NC \-- No Cyber Attacks

#### No [catastrophic]{.underline} cyberattacks.

**Lewis 20** -- James Andrew, senior vice president and director of the
Technology Policy Program at the Center for Strategic and International
Studies. "Dismissing Cyber Catastrophe", Center for Strategic &
International Studies,
https://www.csis.org/analysis/dismissing-cyber-catastrophe, 08-17-2020

A **[[catastrophic cyberattack]{.mark} was]{.underline}** first
**[predicted in the mid-1990s. Since then, predictions]{.underline}** of
a catastrophe **[have appeared regularly]{.underline}** and have entered
the popular consciousness**[.]{.underline}** As a trope, a cyber
catastrophe captures our imagination, but as analysis, **[it
[remains]{.mark} entirely [imaginary]{.mark} and is of dubious value as
a basis for policymaking[.]{.mark}]{.underline}** There has never been a
catastrophic cyberattack.

To qualify as **[a [catastrophe]{.mark}]{.underline}**, an event
**[[must produce]{.mark} damaging [mass]{.mark} effect, including
[casualties]{.mark} and destruction[.]{.mark}]{.underline}** The
**[fires that swept across California]{.underline}** last summer **[were
a catastrophe. Covid]{.underline}**-19 **[has been a
catastrophe]{.underline}**, especially in countries with inadequate
responses**[.]{.underline}** With man-made actions, however, a
catastrophe is harder to produce than it may seem, and **[for
[cyber]{.mark}attacks a [catastrophe requires]{.mark} organizational and
[technical skills]{.mark}]{.underline}** most **[[actors]{.mark} still
[do not possess.]{.mark}]{.underline}** It requires planning,
reconnaissance to find vulnerabilities, and then acquiring or building
attack tools---things that require resources and experience. To achieve
mass effect, either a few central targets (like an electrical grid) need
to be hit or multiple targets would have to be hit simultaneously (as is
the case with urban water systems), something that is itself an
operational challenge.

It is easier to imagine a catastrophe than to produce it. The 2003 East
Coast blackout is the archetype for an attack on the U.S. electrical
grid. No one died in this blackout, and services were restored in a few
days. As electric production is digitized, vulnerability increases, but
many **[electrical companies have made cybersecurity a
priority.]{.underline}** Similarly, at water treatment plants, the
chemicals used to purify water are controlled in ways that make mass
releases difficult. In any case, it would take a massive amount of
chemicals to poison large rivers or lakes, more than most companies keep
on hand, and any release would quickly be diluted.

More importantly, **[there are powerful strategic constraints on those
who have the ability to launch catastrophe attacks. We
have]{.underline}** more than **[two decades of
experience]{.underline}** with the use of cyber techniques and
operations for coercive and criminal purposes and have a clear
understanding of motives, capabilities, and intentions**[. We can be
guided by the]{.underline}** methods of the **[Strategic Bombing
Survey]{.underline}**, which used **[interviews]{.underline}** and
observation (**[rather than hypotheses]{.underline}**) to determine
effect**[.]{.underline}** These methods apply equally to cyberattacks.
The conclusions we can draw from this are:

Nonstate actors and most states lack the capability to launch attacks
that cause physical damage at any level, much less a catastrophe. There
have been regular predictions every year for over a decade that nonstate
actors will acquire these high-end cyber capabilities in two or three
years in what has become a cycle of repetition. The monetary return is
negligible, which dissuades the skilled cybercriminals (mostly Russian
speaking) who might have the necessary skills. One mystery is why these
groups have not been used as mercenaries, and this may reflect either a
degree of control by the Russian state (if it has forbidden mercenary
acts) or a degree of caution by criminals.

**[[There is]{.underline}]{.mark}** enough
[**[uncertainty]{.underline}** **[among potential attackers
about]{.underline}**]{.mark} **[the [U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates'
[ability to attribute]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[[that they are unwilling
to risk]{.mark} massive [retaliation]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[in
response to a catastrophic attack[. (]{.mark}They are perfectly willing
to take the risk of attribution for]{.underline}**
**[espionage]{.underline}** **[and]{.underline}** **[coercive cyber
actions.)]{.underline}**

No one has ever died from a cyberattack, and only a handful of these
attacks have produced physical damage. **[A cyberattack is not a nuclear
weapon, and]{.underline}** **[it is intellectually lazy to equate them
to nuclear weapons.]{.underline}** Using a tactical nuclear weapon
against an urban center would produce several hundred thousand
casualties, while a strategic nuclear exchange would cause tens of
millions of casualties and immense physical destruction. These are
catastrophes that some hack cannot duplicate. The shadow of nuclear war
distorts discussion of cyber warfare.

**[State use of cyber operations is consistent with their broad national
strategies and interests.]{.underline}** **[[Their]{.mark} primary
[emphasis is]{.mark}]{.underline}** on **[[espionage and]{.mark}
political [coercion.]{.mark}]{.underline}** The **[[U]{.mark}nited
[S]{.mark}tates]{.underline}** has **[[opponents]{.underline}]{.mark}**
and is in conflict with them, but they **[[have no interest in]{.mark}
launching [a catastrophic]{.mark} cyber[attack]{.mark}]{.underline}**
**[[since it would]{.mark} certainly [produce]{.mark} an equally
[catastrophic retal]{.mark}iation[.]{.mark}]{.underline}** Their goal is
to stay below the "use-of-force" threshold and undertake damaging cyber
actions against the United States, not start a war.

This has implications for the discussion of **[[inadvertent
escalation]{.underline}]{.mark}**, something that
**[[has]{.underline}]{.mark}** also **[[never
occurred.]{.underline}]{.mark}** The concern over escalation deserves a
longer discussion, as there are both technological and strategic
constraints that shape and limit risk in cyber operations, and the
**[absence of inadvertent escalation [suggests]{.mark} a [high]{.mark}
degree of [control]{.mark} for cyber capabilities [by advanced
states.]{.mark}]{.underline}** [**[Attackers]{.underline}**]{.mark},
particularly **[among the United States' major opponents]{.underline}**
for whom cyber is just one of the tools for confrontation, **[[seek to
avoid]{.mark} actions that could trigger
[escalation.]{.mark}]{.underline}** The United States has two opponents
(China and Russia) who are capable of damaging cyberattacks. **[Russia
has demonstrated its attack skills on the Ukrainian power grid,
but]{.underline}** neither **[Russia]{.underline}** nor China **[would
be well served by a similar attack on the United States.]{.underline}**
Iran is improving and may reach the point where it could use
cyberattacks to cause major damage, but it would only do so when it has
decided to engage in a major armed conflict with the United States. Iran
might attack targets outside the United States and its allies with less
risk and continues to experiment with cyberattacks against Israeli
critical infrastructure. North Korea has not yet developed this kind of
capability.

One major failing of **[catastrophe [scenarios]{.mark}]{.underline}** is
that they **[[discount]{.underline}]{.mark}** the
**[[robustness]{.underline}]{.mark}** and resilience **[[of modern
econ]{.mark}omies.]{.underline}** These economies present multiple
targets and configurations; **[they are harder to damage through
cyberattack than they look, [given]{.mark} the growing]{.underline}**
(albeit incomplete) **[[attention to cybersecurity]{.mark}; [and]{.mark}
experience shows that [people]{.mark} compensate for damage and
[quickly]{.mark} repair or [rebuild.]{.mark}]{.underline}** This was one
of the counterintuitive lessons of the Strategic Bombing Survey. Pre-war
planning assumed that civilian morale and production would crumple under
aerial bombardment. In fact, the opposite occurred. Resistance hardened
and production was restored.1

This is a short overview of why catastrophe is unlikely. Several longer
CSIS reports go into the reasons in some detail. Past performance may
not necessarily predict the future, but **[[after 25 years
without]{.mark} a single catastrophic cyberattack]{.underline}**, we
should invoke the concept cautiously, if at all. **[[Why]{.mark} then,
it is [raised so often?]{.mark}]{.underline}**

Some of the explanation for the emphasis on cyber catastrophe is
hortatory. When the author of one of **[[the first
reports]{.underline}]{.mark}** (**[in the 1990s]{.underline}**) to sound
the alarm over cyber catastrophe was asked later why he had warned of a
cyber Pearl Harbor when it was clear this was not going to happen, his
reply was that he **[[hoped to scare people into
action.]{.underline}]{.mark}** \"Catastrophe is nigh; we must act\" was
possibly a reasonable strategy 22 years ago, but no longer.

The resilience of historical events to remain culturally significant
must be taken into account for an objective assessment of cyber warfare,
and this will require the United States to discard some hypothetical
scenarios. **[[The]{.mark} long experience of living under the [shadow
of nuclear annihilation still shapes]{.mark} American [thinking]{.mark}
and conditions the United States [to expect extreme outcomes.]{.mark}
American thinking is [also]{.mark} shaped [by]{.mark} the experience of
[9/11]{.mark}]{.underline}**, **[a wrenching attack that caught the
United States by surprise[.]{.mark}]{.underline}** Fears of another 9/11
reinforce the memory of nuclear war in driving the catastrophe trope,
but when applied to cyberattack, these scenarios do not track with
operational requirements or the nature of opponent strategy and
planning. The contours of cyber warfare are emerging, but they are not
always what we discuss. Better policy will require greater objectivity.

### 2NC \-- No Cyberattacks

#### No Cyber Impact\-\--Attacks won't escalate\-\--attribution and retaliation deter it, actors don't have the skills to take down anything that matters\-\--companies are bolstering security now, their evidence is 9/11 fear-mongering --OURS is by 25 years of empirical data\-\--that's Lewis

#### \--Self-dampening checks escalation.

**Sørensen and Nyemann 19** -- Heine, Senior Lecturer at the Institute
for Strategy at the Royal Danish Defence College. Dorthe Bach, Senior
Lecturer at the Institute for Strategy, Royal Danish Defence College, US
Joint Forces Command. "Deterrence by Punishment as a Way of Countering
Hybrid Threats -- Why We Need To Go 'Beyond Resilience' in the Gray

<https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/attachment_data/file/783151/20190304-MCDC_CHW_Information_note_-_Deterrence_by_Punishment.pdf>,
03-xx-2019

The Flipside of Deterrence by Punishment -- the Fear of Escalation

[One of the]{.underline} greatest [**reservation**s against
communicating a willingness to take punitive actions concerns the risk
of **escalation** and increased tension due to a more
assertive]{.underline} or offensive [posture. Yet when looking into
possible responses -- retaliation [in **cyber**]{.mark}space, for
example -- a number of [**"self-dampening" mechanisms** appear]{.mark}
to be **[in place]{.mark}** that may be **applicable** to many types of
responses]{.underline}.30 [One example is the requirement to
establish]{.underline} some level of **[attribution]{.underline}** of
aggression on which to base a response. As discussed above, while
attribution is rarely impossible, [it can be a time-consuming and
technically-challenging endeavor. In a high-stakes scenario [the
**time** taken to get **attribution**]{.mark} **as [right]{.mark} as
possible** [means]{.mark} there will be **[plenty]{.mark} of time** to
**[think twice]{.mark}** about actions and consequences, [and]{.mark} to
**[lean on diplomatic measures]{.mark} in parallel**. An example of a
self-dampening mechanism related to the **cyber** domain is the [**large
investment** required to develop]{.mark} **credible [offens]{.mark}ive
capabilities**. Moreover, **an [offens]{.mark}ive cyber capability**
[is]{.mark} **a [transitory]{.mark} tool**]{.underline}31. [The ability
to access a computer system or network to cause harm or damage is **only
[temporary]{.mark}** [and **depend**]{.mark}**ent** [on]{.mark} a **very
[rapid]{.mark} and ongoing [patching of
vulnerabilities]{.mark}**]{.underline}. At the same time, wielding the
cyber instrument despite the downside of "burning" the capacity might
have a de-escalatory effect by communicating capability and credibility
to the opponent with a view to discouraging future hostile attacks.
[[Targeting]{.mark} in any domain]{.underline} -- including cyberspace
-- [[must]{.mark} also [follow]{.mark} **relevant
[rules]{.mark}**]{.underline}, law and due-process [[which]{.mark} will
**self-[limit]{.mark}** the **range** of **[targets]{.mark}** and
**actions** available.]{.underline}

[[A]{.mark} final [**"escalatory showstopper"** is]{.mark} related to
the challenge of **identifying** and **developing targets** of adequate
strategic significance -- **not too much, not too little** -- to achieve
the desired effect. Responding to hybrid aggression by applying
**["middle range"]{.mark} punitive [actions]{.mark}** that are
**[proportionate]{.mark}** to the aggression threatened or suffered
[will]{.mark} also **self-[limit]{.mark}** the escalatory potential. [It
is **unlikely**]{.mark} to be [in the **interest** of **any**]{.mark}
hybrid aggressor [to pursue **a**]{.mark}**n escalatory
[spiral]{.mark}** above and beyond where they were looking to compete in
the first place: on the hybrid level]{.underline}. Nevertheless, one way
to mitigate the risk of escalation -- while enhancing civilian oversight
and interagency coordination -- would be to establish rules of
engagement for punitive actions on the hybrid level32 . This would
provide decision-makers with common guidelines to pursue punitive
actions that fall below the "use of force threshold". Moreover, this
could actually bolster the credibility of punitive actions by signaling
to hybrid aggressors the intent to take pre-prepared punitive actions
when deemed necessary: in other words, a "playbook" for countering
hybrid threats.

#### \--[Reversible consequences]{.underline} mean attacks don't provoke war. 

**Borghard and Lonergan 19** -- Erica, Assistant Professor at the Army

Limited Costliness of Offensive Cyber Effects

Even under circumstances in which a state may possess the right cyber
response capabilities at the desired time, [its [response may not
generate]{.mark} **[sufficient costs]{.mark}** against the target [to be
**perceived as escalatory**]{.mark}]{.underline}.41 Fundamental limits
on the cost-generation potential of offensive cyber operations stem from
the fact that [cyber capabilities lack the **physical violence** of
conventional and nuclear ones.]{.underline} [Cyber weapons target
**data**; they disrupt, manipulate, degrade, or destroy data resident on
networks and systems or in transit]{.underline}.42 Moreover, [aside from
those cyber capabilities that permanently destroy data and for which
there are no backups to which a target can revert, [cyber effects are
**temporary** and]{.mark} often **[reversible]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

[The utility of military instruments of power for the purposes of
coercion or brute force inheres in their abilities to
inflict]{.underline}---or credibly threaten to inflict---[**significant
damage** and **harm** against a target state]{.underline} (its civilian
population or its military forces) [to achieve a political
objective]{.underline}.43 [Cyber weapons]{.underline} could be (and have
been) used to disrupt an adversary's networks and systems---overwhelming
them such that they temporarily lose the ability to function or the
target loses confidence in their reliability---or [even to produce
destructive effects by destroying data resident on these
systems]{.underline} or, in rarer circumstances, [producing effects in
the physical realm.]{.underline}44 While conducting multiple
cyberattacks against a targeted state's critical national
infrastructure, for example, could in theory generate significant
economic and national security consequences, [the **temporal aspects**
of offensive cyber]{.underline} [operations as described above **limit**
the ability of even the most **capable states** to sustain persistent,
high-cost effects against **multiple strategic targets**]{.underline}
over time. [There is simply **no guarantee** that a state can generate
**significant costs** against a target in th]{.underline}e context of an
[unfolding crisis]{.underline}. This reality starkly contrasts with the
relative predictability and reliability of conventional effects. Indeed,
the empirical record has largely validated this claim; "[[the **vast
majority** of **malicious cyber activity** has taken place far below the
**threshold of armed conflict**]{.mark} between states, and has not
risen to the level that would]{.underline} [trigger such a
conflict]{.underline}."45 This is why, in Lin's parlance, "[going cyber
is **pre-escalatory**" and **[countervalue
cyberattacks]{.mark}**]{.underline} (those that target civilian, rather
than military, assets) [[occur "**all the time** now and are at the
**BOTTOM** of the escalation ladder]{.underline}]{.mark}" \[emphasis in
original\].46 Rather than their ability to wreak permanent, destructive
effects, [cyber operations are often prized for their **temporary** and
**reversible nature**]{.underline}.47

[One metric to assess the cost-generation potential of offensive cyber
is in terms of loss of life]{.underline}. By this measure, [cyber
operations are **unlikely** to inflict significant harm]{.underline}.
While theoretically possible that cyber operations could lead directly
to a loss of life, [[no one has reportedly **died to date** as a
direct]{.mark} [**result of a cyberattack** despite over **30 years** of
recorded cyber operations]{.mark}]{.underline}.48 Even in hypothetical
catastrophic scenarios, [the cost in terms of human casualties is
**minimal**.]{.underline} For instance, [common worst-case scenarios of
cyberattacks revolve around the **loss of power** stemming from a
cyberattack]{.underline} on [an **electric grid**]{.underline}.49
However, even in this instance, [the conceivable damage from the loss of
power over an **extended period** is **far less** than that which could
be wreaked using **basic, limited conventional
capabilities**]{.underline}. To draw a comparison, when Hurricane Sandy
hit the United States' eastern seaboard in late October 2012, over 8.5
million people were left without power---with many going weeks and even
months before it was brought back online.50 [Yet a US National Hurricane
Center postmortem of Hurricane Sandy reported]{.underline} that of the
159 people in the United States killed either directly or indirectly,
only "[**about 50 of these deaths** were the result of extended power
outages during **cold weather**, which led to deaths from
**hypothermia**]{.underline}, falls in the dark by senior citizens, [or
**carbon monoxide** poisoning from improperly placed
generators]{.underline} or cooking devices."51 [If a cyberattack took
out power of a similar magnitude and duration of Hurricane Sandy, it is
conceivable that an **equivalent number** of casualties would result.
[The 2015 **synchronized cyberattacks** against **Ukrainian power
companies**]{.mark}, attributed to Russia]{.underline}, was the first
known example of an offensive cyber operation targeting a state's power
grid. [Its cost was ultimately low---service was temporarily [disrupted
to 225,000 customers]{.mark} for several hours, and energy providers
operated at a limited capacity for some time after service]{.underline}
was restored.52 [[There were no **reported
casualties**]{.underline}]{.mark} from this power outage. While any
casualty resulting from a cyberattack would certainly be lamentable[,
even **worst-case scenario** figures are **minor** in comparison to the
cost in **human lives** stemming from other, **even limited**, kinetic
military operations.]{.underline}

## Advantage -- Arctic War 

### 1NC \-- AT: Arctic Shipping

#### Arctic navigation is prohibitively dangerous 

**Lin 23** (Bowen Lin, PhD candidate in engineering at Intelligent
Transportation Systems Research Center and National Engineering Research
Center for Water Transport Safety, Wuhan University of Technology;
Mao Zheng, Xiumin Chu, Wengang Mao, Daiyong Zhang, Mingyang Zhang,
06-27-2025, \"An overview of scholarly literature on navigation hazards
in Arctic shipping routes\", Environmental Science and Pollution
Research (2024) 31:40419--40435 ,
https://doi.org/10.1007/s11356-023-29050-2) -- DOA: 6/25/2025, MoState
JG

Difficulties and risks when ships navigating in Arctic shipping routes

[[Different from traditional shipping]{.mark} routes, the [difficulties,
and risks of]{.mark} ship [navigation in Arctic shipping]{.mark} routes
[are]{.mark} significantly [increased]{.mark} due to the unique
environment such as low temperature, sea ice, and high
latitude]{.underline} (Zhang et al., 2017). The difficulties and risks
of ship navigation in the Arctic shipping routes are analyzed with
regard to the various disturbing factors in the Arctic shipping routes
(shown in Fig. 9).

[Low temperature]{.underline} factors

The [**[extremely low temp]{.mark}**erature]{.underline} in the Arctic
shipping routes will [[affect]{.mark} the [crew]{.mark} psychologically
and physically to various degrees]{.underline}. In a low-temperature
environment, [crew members become easily fatigued.]{.underline} The
activity level of crew decreases significantly, and the perception and
response speed of external conditions also decrease (Sun, 2020; Zhang et
al., 2006).

[Under the effect of sea waves, rain, and snow, the ship [deck
and]{.mark} the outer [hull]{.mark} are easy to [accumulate
water]{.mark}]{.underline}. Affected by low-temperature conditions,
water on deck, hull and equipment will freeze quickly. If those
accumulated water is not treated in time and that will affect the
stability of the ship and cause the radar and other facilities to be
damaged, which will eventually cause safety risks. At the same time, the
toughness of steel and other materials also decreases at low
temperature. Under the influence of continuous low temperature during
the voyage, the equipment of the ship engine room and piping system
becomes extremely vulnerable (Ma, 2015). If no auxiliary heating
equipment or deicing equipment is equipped, the [[maintenance
cost]{.mark} of the ship's equipment will [increase]{.mark}
significantly]{.underline}. [The toughness of a ship's hull also
decreases as the temperature drops. Therefore, when a ship collides at
low temperatures, the [danger and hazards are]{.mark} dramatically
[increased]{.mark}]{.underline} (Zhang et al. 2023a).

In terms of cargo ships, which own the largest proportion of ships
navigate in Arctic shipping routes, some types of cargo are difficult to
store or transport at low temperature. Consequently, cargo ships should
consider the types of cargo to be transported in advance to avoid
economic losses when choosing Arctic shipping routes.

[Sea ice factors]{.underline}

Affected by the low temperature, the sea [ice concentration and
thickness in the Arctic Ocean are high]{.underline}. [[When ships
navigate in]{.mark} the highly concentrated [sea ice]{.mark}
area]{.underline}, the [ships' sailing [speed reduces]{.mark}
significantly [and]{.mark} the [maneuvering]{.mark} performance is
[decreased]{.mark}]{.underline} (Buysse, 2007). Additionally, under the
influence of strong winds and currents, the [[dangers posed by
floating]{.mark} sea [ice]{.mark} to ship navigation [are]{.mark}
further [increased]{.mark} and the sea ice becomes one of the biggest
disturbing factors for ships sailing in the Arctic shipping
routes]{.underline} (Li et al., 2018; Xu et al., 2022).
[Collisions]{.underline} between ships and sea ice [are]{.underline} one
of the most [important]{.underline} accidents in Arctic shipping routes
(Fu et al., 2021). It is generally recognized that general ships can
safely pass through according to the original calculation when the
concentration of sea ice in the shipping routes is less than 30% and the
thickness of sea ice is less than 30cm (Zhang, 2016). In the area with
high concentrations and thickness of sea ice, [low ice class ships are
prone to ice trap accidents]{.underline}. [[Even when sailing
under]{.mark} the escort of [icebreakers]{.mark}, there are still in
danger of accidents such as enclosure by sea ice on both sides of the
secondary shipping routes, grounding, damage to the hull by squeezing,
or damage to the propeller by inhalation of broken ice while
astern]{.underline}.

When the ship is trapped by sea ice, the steering ability becomes worse
and it is easy to move with the floating ice and cause the ship
grounding. At the same time, sea ice on either side of a ship can crush
the fragile sides, causing [hull damage]{.underline} (Bai et al., 2022;
Zhang et al., 2023b). Sea [[ice]{.underline}]{.mark} [can]{.underline}
also [[block navigation]{.mark} signs]{.underline} and cause hull
tremors, which can [greatly affect shipping navigation.]{.underline} In
addition, it also leads to [severe disruption of ships' transit
schedules and causes economic loss]{.underline} (Turnbull et al. 2019).
In order to assess the navigation risks of ships under different sea ice
conditions, the Polar Operational Limit Assessment Risk Indexing System
(POLARIS) was proposed by the International Maritime Organization (IMO)
in 2016 to assess the navigational risks of ships in all kinds of ice
conditions.

POLARIS introduced the risk value index (RV) to assess the risk of ship
navigation in the sea ice cover area. The ship navigation risk index RV
is determined by the ship\'s ice resistance and sea ice type, its value
ranges from −6 to 3. If RV is negative, the navigation risk of the ship
is higher, which means it is not suitable for navigation and the smaller
the value, the higher the navigation risk is. When RV is positive, the
navigation risk is lower and the larger the value is, the higher the
navigation safety is.

POLARIS divides ships into three categories based on their ice-breaking
capacity, called Class A, B, and C. Among them, Class A ships meet the
ice-breaking capacity under relatively severe ice conditions, and the
ice-breaking thickness is generally over 70cm. Class B ships refer to
those ships that can sail independently in thin ice (typical thickness
of approximately 30cm), and the design standard is not lower than the
safety design standard of PC7 class icebreaker of the International
Shipping Society; Class C ships cover other ships that can operate
within polar waters. Ships of this class can only sail in open waters or
under extremely thin sea ice cover conditions and mainly include ships
of the Finnish-Swedish classifcation types 1A Super, 1A, 1B, IC, and
general merchant ships. RV values under diferent sea ice thicknesses are
shown in Table 4. Combined with the RV value and regional sea ice
concentration, the comprehensive navigation risk calculation method is
shown in Equation 1. RIOV = (1) ∑CT × RVV,T

Among them, RIOV is a comprehensive navigation risk index; CT is the
concentration of type T sea ice in the channel; RVV,T is the RV value
for a Type V ship under Type T sea ice. According to the comprehensive
navigation risk index, ship operations can be divided into normal
operation, elevated operation risk, and operation subject to special
consideration. Table 5 shows the ship operations under diferent risk
indices. Icebreaker escort is generally required when the risk index is
negative and the RIO result under escort can be recognized as the
original RIO result plus 10. If ships are of elevated operation risk,
the recommended speed is shown in Table 6.

[Poor visibility factors]{.underline}

The [**[frequency of]{.mark}** advection **[fog]{.mark}** in the Arctic
Ocean in summer **[is]{.mark}** extremely
**[high]{.mark}**]{.underline}, so Arctic [shipping routes are usually
covered by sea fog and ships often have poor visibility
effects]{.underline}. Stratospheric fog in the Arctic region is
characterized by a [fast appearance and long duration.]{.underline}
Moreover, the Arctic region is covered by a large amount of sea ice and
snow; the [crew is prone to [snow blindness]{.mark} which
[affects]{.mark} the [perception]{.mark} and judgment ability of the
surrounding situation and the risk of major accidents increases
significantly]{.underline} (Goerlandt et al., 2017). Additionally,
[visibility deteriorates further under the influence of polar nights
caused by high latitudes.]{.underline} Studies have shown that ship
navigation has potential safety hazards when the visibility is less than
4km. When it is lower than 1km, the probability of accidents increases
significantly (Lv, 2012). Affected by various factors, sometimes the
visibility of ships on the Arctic shipping routes is even lower than 100
meters, which seriously affects the safe navigation of ships.

[Communication factors]{.underline}

[Ship communication failures are one of the leading causes of ship
collisions in Arctic shipping routes]{.underline} (Fu et al., 2021; Xu
et al., 2022). The [Arctic region is affected by the high latitude
effect and harsh environment; as a result, the **[accuracy and
timeliness of equipment]{.mark}** such as magnetic compass longitude
**[is]{.mark}** greatly **[reduced]{.mark}**]{.underline}. The
[[coverage of]{.mark} polar [communication and]{.mark} meteorological
[data collection]{.mark} facilities [is]{.mark} also
[low]{.mark}]{.underline}. Moreover, [under low temperatures, mobile
data collection equipment such as drones is restricted in their work and
data collection measures are limited]{.underline} (Ma et al., 2022).
Accompanied by the fact that the ice foe is constantly moving under the
influence of wind, [it is difficult for ships to communicate, perceive,
and predict the surrounding environment in real-time, which affects ship
navigation significantly]{.underline} (Zhong, 2013; Zhu et al., 2015b).

[Lack of infrastructure]{.underline}

The [**transport and [logistics infrastructure]{.mark} in the Arctic
shipping routes [is underdeveloped]{.mark}** and the digitization of
basic services required for safe navigation in difficult conditions is
insufficient]{.underline} (Vicentiy, 2021). When ships navigate in the
Arctic shipping routes, [it is difficult to get supplies from the
nearest port when ships are far inland.]{.underline} Moreover, due to
the harsh natural environment, [there are few ports and a lack of
infrastructure]{.underline} along the Arctic shipping routes,
[which]{.underline} further [increases]{.underline} the [difficulty of
obtaining supplies and rescue]{.underline} for ships. Therefore, the
[capability of search and rescue is low]{.underline} in the Arctic,
which means that the consequences of ship (especially for oil tankers)
accidents resulting in oil spills may be very serious (Afenyo et al.,
2019; Kujala et al., 2019; Nevalainen et al., 2018; Xu et al., 2021).

[Lack of navigation experience]{.underline}

According to statistics on Arctic shipping routes accidents, about [75%
of accidents are caused by humans]{.underline}. In general, the number
of ships currently navigating in the Arctic shipping routes is lower
than in traditional shipping routes. [Since]{.underline} most of the
[[crew]{.underline}]{.mark} members [[lack experience]{.mark} in
navigating the Arctic shipping routes]{.underline}, the [possibility of
accidents under complex operations increases]{.underline}. Therefore,
crew training should be strengthened and experienced crew members need
to be screened before Arctic navigation in order to reduce the risk of
accidents (Boström et al., 2017; IMO, 2014; Xu et al., 2022).

[Lack of historical data]{.underline}

Large amounts of historical data are one of the key foundations for
conducting research in the field of transport (Chen et al., 2020). As a
shipping route that is still in the exploration period, the [historical
data]{.underline} of ships navigating in Arctic shipping routes
currently preserved is not enough and [is scattered]{.underline} in
different routes (Xu et al., 2021), and there are few studies related to
the analysis of navigation environment and traffic simulation of Arctic
shipping routes using ship navigation big data (Ma, 2013).

[[High risk of collision]{.mark}]{.underline}

The [collision between icebreakers and escorted ships is the most common
accident in polar ship formation navigation]{.underline} (Goerland et
al., 2016; Nevalainen et al., 2017; Zhang et al., 2017), [which can
[lead to]{.mark} injuries and human [casualties]{.mark}]{.underline}
(Kum et al.,. 2015). Therefore, reasonable ship spacing should be
selected to avoid the collision risk of ship formation. The distance
between ships in formation is determined by the captain of the
icebreaker. When ships sailing with the icebreaker, a large distance
between ships should be adopted to avoid the risk of ship collision in
an emergency, and a small distance between ships should be adopted to
avoid the secondary shipping routes opened by the icebreaker being
covered by sea ice again and eventually causing ice trap. Comprehensive
analysis of the risk of collision between ships and other ships and sea
ice to determine the safe following distance (Liu et al., 2023). The
[following distance of ships varies greatly under different ice
conditions.]{.underline} When ships sail in open waters, the distance
between ships is often kept several hundred meters to avoid collision.
[In some waters with high sea ice pressure, the secondary shipping
routes opened by icebreakers will be re-covered by squeezed sea ice in a
short time, and the [sailing distance]{.mark} of ship formation is only
a few meters]{.underline} (Li et al., 2021; Wan et al., 2020; Wu, 2016;
Xuan et al., 2014), [significantly [increased risk]{.mark} of
collision.]{.underline}

[Complex navigation condition]{.underline}

[In some water areas of the Arctic shipping routes (especially the
Northwest Passage), the [topography]{.mark} of the route [is
complicated]{.mark}]{.underline}, the [width is narrow, and the water
depth is shallow due to the interference of sea ice]{.underline}, and
[ships need to turn or adjust speed several times to prevent collision
or ice trapping]{.underline}. [[Due to]{.mark} the [shallow]{.mark}
water effect [and bank effect]{.mark}, and the difficulty of ship
operation when navigating in ice areas]{.underline}, [[accidents
are]{.mark} very [likely]{.mark} to happen]{.underline}. Besides,
navigating in the ice-covered area sometimes requires frequent rudder
operation so the crew has to analyze various hydrometeorological
information in time and look for the passage between the ice, which
causes a [great labor intensity and pressure]{.underline}. In polar
voyage, the work should be timely adjusted according to the status of
the crew, so as to reduce the influence of crew fatigue on the
operational level of the ship.

[Other factors]{.underline}

The opening period of the Arctic shipping route is not fixed due to the
influence of meteorological conditions such as temperature in different
years. Therefore, the proportion of general cargo ships, bulk carriers,
and other ships in the Arctic shipping routes is large. [[Container
ships]{.underline}]{.mark} have higher requirements on shipping dates
and timeliness of delivery of goods, so those kinds of ships [[have
fewer navigation advantages in choosing the Arctic]{.mark} shipping
routes.]{.underline}

#### And too expensive: ship costs and safety 

**Lin 23** (Bowen Lin, PhD candidate in engineering at Intelligent
Transportation Systems Research Center and National Engineering Research
Center for Water Transport Safety, Wuhan University of Technology;
Mao Zheng, Xiumin Chu, Wengang Mao, Daiyong Zhang, Mingyang Zhang,
06-27-2025, \"An overview of scholarly literature on navigation hazards
in Arctic shipping routes\", Environmental Science and Pollution
Research (2024) 31:40419--40435 ,
https://doi.org/10.1007/s11356-023-29050-2) -- DOA: 6/25/2025, MoState
JG

The cost of ocean voyage in Arctic shipping routes

The cost of an ocean voyage mainly includes fuel cost, marine insurance
cost, ship depreciation cost, staff salary cost, and other costs (shown
in Fig. 8). [[Compared with other routes]{.mark}, the **[Arctic
shipping]{.mark} routes** may also **[incur additional costs]{.mark}**
such as pilotage cost, icebreaker escort cist, and sea ice situation
forecast cost]{.underline} (Zhang et al., 2009).

(1) [Fuel cost]{.underline}

Fuel cost is the most important factor that affect shipping costs,
accounting for about half of the total shipping cost (Chen et al. 2022;
Fei et al. 2020; Liu, 2008). Fuel costs are mainly related to mileage
and fuel prices. According to the statistical report, each \$1 increase
in fuel prices increases shipping costs by an average of 1%. Based on
the fuel cost advantage, the Arctic shipping routes are typically more
than 25% cheaper than traditional shipping routes, and the higher the
price of oil, the more pronounced the advantage of the Arctic shipping
routes.

(2) [Marine insurance cost]{.underline}

Marine insurance cost generally accounts for 1%\~3% of the total cost of
freight value, including Marine risk protection insurance and external
risk insurance costs. On the traditional ocean routes between China and
Europe, piracy activities are frequent through the Strait of Malacca and
Somali shipping routes and the insurance cost is super high. The
insurance cost related to piracy prevention alone accounts for
0.125%\~0.2% of the total cost of cargo value (Li, 2009). The adoption
of the Arctic shipping routes has a lower probability of piracy and
other risks due to the poor sea ice condition and low temperature; as a
result, the insurance cost is lower than the traditional route.

(3) [Ship depreciation cost]{.underline}

[[Seaworthy ships on the Arctic]{.mark} shipping routes [need to be
equipped with additional capabilities]{.mark} such as ice resistance,
[and the **cost of the ships is much higher**]{.mark} than that of
general cargo ships]{.underline}. The [cost of Arctic ships with similar
magnitude is **[more than 20%]{.mark} higher** [than]{.mark} that of
[traditional]{.mark} ocean-going [cargo ships]{.mark}]{.underline}. At
the same time, due to the influence of low temperature, sea ice, or
other factors, the [[wear and consumption]{.mark} of ships in the Arctic
shipping routes are [accelerated, so]{.mark} the [depreciation and
maintenance costs]{.mark} of ships [are higher]{.mark} than those of
other ships, accounting for 14.7% of the shipping cost
approximately]{.underline} (Zhu, 2007). With the increasing temperature
in the Arctic and the melting of sea ice, the navigation conditions in
Arctic shipping routes are improving year by year. In the future, the
depreciation cost of ships operating in the Arctic shipping routes will
be reduced and eventually approach the traditional shipping routes.

(4) Staff salary cost

Staff salary accounts for about 5.3% of the total maritime
transportation cost (Zhu, 2007), which is mainly related to personnel
salary and voyage date. The distance of the Arctic shipping routes is
20\~50% lower than that of the traditional shipping routes, with the
shipping schedule of different routes being shortened by 3\~14.5 days on
average. Meanwhile, the queuing time of the Arctic shipping routes is
much lower than that of the traditional shipping routes, and the
shipping schedule is further shortened. Staff salaries and
administrative costs are reduced by 1.6%\~2.7% on average.

(5) Other cost

Currently, the Arctic shipping routes are not limited by tonnage, which
makes it ideal for mega-ships and superjumbos to navigate. As the
transport cost per unit mass of large tonnage ships is lower than that
of small tonnage ships, the average freight cost of ships in the Arctic
shipping routes is lower than that of traditional shipping routes. [Due
to the influence of sea ice, [some]{.mark} ships [need]{.mark} to be
assisted by pilotage or [icebreaker escorts]{.mark} when crossing Arctic
shipping routes. Furthermore, [it is difficult]{.mark} [to]{.mark}
rescue or [deal with accidents]{.mark} in the Arctic shipping routes and
[resulting in additional]{.mark} navigation [costs]{.mark}]{.underline}.

### 1NC \-- AT: Arctic War

#### The Arctic is stable and politically insulated from warming 

**Valberg 24** (Anna Valberg, M.A. in Political Science from University
of Oslo, Head of communications at Fridtjof Nansen Institute. 1-26-2024,
\"War in the Arctic? Researchers debunk three myths about the High
North\", Sciencenorway.no,
https://partner.sciencenorway.no/arctic-climate-conflict/war-in-the-arctic-researchers-debunk-three-myths-about-the-high-north/2314629)
-- DOA: 6/7/25, MoState JG

What could lead to a serious conflict in the Arctic? A story from nearly
20 years ago might provide some clues. It was a cold autumn and the sea
was rough. Norwegian Coast Guard inspectors were on duty and were far
out to sea, about to board the Russian trawler Electron, as they had
noticed the vessel was not abiding by protocols. What followed next put
Norwegian diplomacy to the test. The Russian fishermen took the
Norwegian inspectors into Russian waters, and were pursued by Norwegian
Coast Guard ships and helicopters. It later emerged that the Norwegian
Naval Special Operations Command had also been mobilised. Could have
ended differently today After delicate diplomatic efforts, Norway
managed to get the Russian authorities to instruct the trawler to
release the Norwegians and hand over Electron's vessel documents. The
Coast Guard is part of the Norwegian defence. To avoid escalating the
conflict, it was crucial that the Russian Northern Fleet was kept out of
the area. "The Electron incident occurred in 2005. If this had happened
in 2024, it\'s not certain it would have ended as well," Andreas
Østhagen says. He is a senior researcher at the Fridtjof Nansen
Institute (FNI). He studies Arctic geopolitics, seeking to identify
factors that could lead to dangerous security flare ups in the Arctic.
Everything that could have gone wrong "When thinking about potential
conflict in the North, it\'s precisely a scenario like the Electron case
that could spiral out of control," Østhagen says. What would have
happened if the fishery inspectors were armed? What if the weather had
been good enough for the marines to board the ship? What if the admirals
of the Russian Northern Fleet were not interested in de-escalation? Many
things could have gone wrong, contributing to an escalation of the
conflict rather than keeping the tumult at as low a level as possible.
Østhagen envisions two scenarios for conflict in the North: A low-scale
incident that escalates or a full-blown crisis, which, in that case,
would be linked to something much larger than the Arctic where Russia
plays a key role. He references the situation in Ukraine and the
currenty global instability. "There is no doubt that interest in the
Arctic is increasing, both commercially and in terms of a geopolitical
power play," he says. Geopolitics deals with how a country\'s politics,
history, and society are shaped based on its geography.

**[The situation is calmer than expected]{.underline}** But Østhagen
believes there are three major misconceptions about geopolitics in the
Arctic: The first is the possibility of war in the Arctic. The second is
that there are unresolved borders in the Arctic and that a resource race
is underway. The third is that climate change is the primary catalyst
for all changes in the Arctic region. "The [reality is much
calmer]{.underline}," Østhagen says. "The [Electron case illustrates
well everything that can go wrong. But it also shows why]{.underline}
**[[things usually don't go wrong. There\'s a willingness to
cooperate]{.underline}]{.mark}**,\" Svein Vigeland Rottem says. [He is a
senior researcher at FNI and studies cooperation in the
Arctic]{.underline}. Rottem and Østhagen do not agree that the Arctic is
a powder keg and a dangerous place, as recently stated by Lars Saunes,
former head of the Norwegian Navy, to Norwegian Broadcasting Corporation
NRK. [Myth 1: The possibility of war in the Arctic]{.underline}
Østhagen, addressing the misconception of imminent war in the Arctic,
explains that the [[conditions in the Arctic are]{.mark} often more
[stable and transparent]{.mark} than many assume]{.underline}.
[**[Conflict is not easily triggered]{.underline}**,
[considering]{.underline}]{.mark} [the vast areas and diverse countries
involved]{.underline}. Østhagen emphasises the **[[enormity of the
Arctic,]{.underline}]{.mark}** which [encompasses eight countries, four
million people, and extensive ocean areas. The countries include Russia,
the Nordic states, the USA, and Canada.]{.underline} He notes that the
[significant Russian presence]{.underline} -- with almost half of the
Arctic landmass under Russian control -- [influences perceptions of
geopolitics in the North]{.underline}. [Iver B. Neumann, a Russia
researcher and director at the FNI, concurs that **the Arctic is [less
likely to witness]{.mark} armed [conflict than other]{.mark} global
[locations]{.mark}**.]{.underline} He believes the primary driver for
military conflict in the Arctic is Russia\'s use of Murmansk as a base
for nuclear weapons. "There are three main ways to keep such weapons
mobile: in aircraft, on land by rail, and at sea on submarines. Where
such submarines exist, as in Murmansk, there is also significant
military attention. And where there is military attention, there is also
the potential for conflict," he says. Neumann advises caution in
responding to Russia\'s desire for global attention and military
control, stressing the importance of Norway maintaining a cool head,
enforcing sovereignty, and not succumbing to intimidation. [Myth 2:
Unresolved borders and a race for resources]{.underline} As the Arctic
becomes more accessible due to melting sea ice because of climate
change, [a common myth suggests a scramble for resources and land in the
North will occur]{.underline}. But Østhagen clarifies that, [unlike in
other parts of the world, [there are almost **no disputes over Arctic
borders**]{.mark}**.**]{.underline} "All the borders are settled," he
says, noting that [the last conflict between Canada and Greenland was
resolved in 2022.]{.underline} [[Maritime boundaries are key]{.mark} for
resource management and potential conflict]{.underline}. Law of the sea
grants coastal states sovereign rights for resource exploitation in
their economic zones. In the Arctic, [[only one]{.mark} maritime
boundary [remains unresolved]{.mark}. It is [between the USA and
Canada]{.mark} -- two [close allies]{.mark}]{.underline}. [Østhagen, who
wrote his PhD on maritime disputes, foresees [**no major concerns**
regarding]{.mark} sovereignty or [territorial
issues]{.mark}]{.underline}. He believes the [myth persists due to
historical reasons but]{.underline} emphasises that, with all the land
borders settled and just one disputed sea border, [**[Arctic resources
are already distributed]{.mark}** among the eight Arctic
states.]{.underline} [Myth 3: Climate change drives all shifts in the
Arctic [A]{.mark} third [myth suggests]{.mark} that [climate change is
the]{.mark} main [driver]{.mark} for all political shifts in the
Arctic]{.underline}. [But Østhagen **[caution]{.mark}**s [against
oversimplifying]{.mark} the issue]{.underline}, noting that while
climate change does affect fish migration -- leading to potential future
conflicts around resource-rich areas like Svalbard -- it\'s important to
be specific about which aspects of climate change increase conflict
risks. Another claim Østhagen addresses is [the notion that Russia's
military buildup in the Arctic is climate change-driven]{.underline}. He
suggests that this [is only partially true]{.underline}, as Russia is
generally strengthening its military. The Arctic, traditionally seen as
secure due to inaccessibility and ice cover, hosts many of Russia's
nuclear weapons. "Putin's aim to rebuild Russia as a military
superpower, with credible nuclear deterrence, involves deploying weapons
to strategic locations like the Kola Peninsula," Østhagen says. With
climate changes, increased shipping activity in Russian Arctic waters
becomes more feasible. "Therefore, it is perhaps not surprising that
Russia wants to maintain control over its northern territories," the
researcher says. However, according to Østhagen, [[Russia's military
buildup]{.mark} in the region [is more about]{.mark} relations with
[NATO than]{.mark} the changing [climate]{.mark}]{.underline}.

#### No arctic geopolitical scramble

Marja Helena **Sivonen**, 20**25** **SYKE** Finnish Environment
Institute, Climate Solutions Unit - University of Tampere, Faculty of
Social Sciences, & Paula Kivimaa - SPRU, University of Sussex
\"Interrelations between security and the zero‑carbon energy transition
in the Finnish and Norwegian Arctic.\" Energy Research & Social Science
119 (2025): 103841.
<https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S2214629624004328>
(ermo-MSDI, acc 6-22-2025)

Norway defines the Arctic as the areas between the Arctic Circle and the
North Pole. Although the region has remained stable for a long time,
[[the most recent Norwegian]{.underline}]{.mark} government Arctic
[[Policy]{.underline}]{.mark} \[59\] clearly [[states that Russian
military activity is a threat and that Norway relies on a strong NATO
presence in the area.]{.underline}]{.mark} Arctic issues are at the core
of the country\'s foreign policy; almost half a million of its citizens
live in the area. Although it is not densely populated, it covers a
[[third of Norway\'s territory]{.underline}]{.mark} and most of its
maritime exclusive economic zone. It is also rather well integrated with
the rest of the country in practice \[60\]. Officially, the most
commonly used term [[is "High North]{.underline}]{.mark}", which comes
from the Norwegian word nordområdene (officially "the northern regions",
but in English "High North" is mostly used to refer to northern areas in
connection to foreign policy and international relations, especially
Svalbard, and the states around the Barents Sea). Russian relations are
an important factor in this context \[61\]. The politically loaded term
'High North' is connected to "efforts to safeguard Norwegian interests
through various initiatives and cross-border cooperation in the North
Calotte region and the Barents region as a whole" \[59\]. Interest in
developing a more robust Arctic policy has grown in recent years in
Norway because of climate change, which allows more activity in the
polar areas, technological development and increasing commercial
activity \[62\]. At the same time, [[there is no "scramble for the
Arctic", despite the media claims]{.underline}]{.mark} \[63\]. [[The
area has been characterised by political stability and well-functioning
regimes]{.underline}]{.mark} \[62\].

### 2NC \-- AT: Arctic War

#### \-\--The Arctic is stable; maritime boundary disputes only occur between allies, resources are distributed and climate is a non-factor -- Valberg 

#### It will never escalate; resource access too expensive, and military posture is defensive 

**Pincus 24** (Rebecca Pincus, Ph.D. in Natural Resources & Energy
Security from the University of Vermont, MSEL and MSNR in Environmental
Law and Energy Security, BS in Foreign Service from Georgetown
University, Director of the Polar Institute at The Wilson Center, Former
Assistant Professor at the Naval War College, former Arctic and Climate
Strategy Advisor in the Office of the Secretary of Defense for Policy, ,
5-3-2024, \"Small Ocean, Big Hype: Arctic Myths and Realities\", War on
the Rocks,
https://warontherocks.com/2024/05/small-ocean-big-hype-arctic-myths-and-realities/)
-- DOA: 6/21/25, MoState JG

The Arctic Ocean may be the world's smallest but it looms large in the
imagination. Remote and unique, the Arctic is, for some at least, the
most romanticized ocean in the world. It has been fueling legends and
ambitions for centuries, and continues to fuel new geopolitical myths
today.

[Global dynamics, from climate change to the decline of American
hegemony and tech]{.underline}nological revolution, [are reshaping the
Arctic.]{.underline} Unfortunately, [mythmaking about the Arctic
continues to distort the narratives available to both public and elite
audiences.]{.underline} Heather Exner-Pirot beautifully explains how
easy this can be and, as Josh Tallis has written, the phantom of Arctic
misgovernance can be used to raise alarm about an unusual gap in Arctic
security governance. In reality, though, the Arctic is profoundly
normal.

The following is an effort to disassemble the leading myths about the
Arctic Ocean and to underscore nuggets of truth underlying the legends.
The natural tendency, noted above, for people to process their
understanding of the Arctic through the most dramatic filter possible,
is not easy to counteract by listing mundane realities. But falling for
the hype is not a good way to make policy. Refuting these myths can put
the focus back on more mundane, but ultimately more valuable, solutions.

The Legendary Arctic Scramble

Most of the [modern Arctic myths and legends are spicing up mundane
realities with added drama]{.underline}. For example, it's [common to
see the Arctic referred to as a battleground for valuable natural
resources.]{.underline} It's the "\$1 Trillion Ocean", [holding massive
reserves of oil and gas]{.underline} (or one trillion dollars' worth of
critical minerals, take your pick), [with echoes of Dr. Evil.
Competition over resources is framed as a "race" or "battle," although
it is often unclear what winning might mean.]{.underline} Industry and
science have long known that the Arctic holds a significant endowment of
natural resources. **[Prospecting and [development in the Arctic lag
behind]{.mark} that of [other]{.mark} [regions due to]{.mark} the
significant [logistical challenges and]{.mark} resulting [extra
costs]{.mark} relating to arctic operations]{.underline}**. [[Resource
deposits]{.mark} in the Arctic [must be of a higher grade or size to
attract investors and overcome]{.mark} the added [risk]{.mark} to
capital.]{.underline} In short, [it has historically been [cheaper to
look elsewhere]{.mark}.]{.underline} And that largely remains true:
[[There has not been a flood of]{.mark} investment and new
[development]{.mark} into the Arctic.]{.underline}

Rather than unclaimed, ungoverned space, the Arctic Ocean and
surrounding landmass are almost entirely sovereign lands and waters of
the eight Arctic states. Yes, there is a small area of high seas, the
central Arctic Ocean. This area is under a fishing moratorium and can
only be accessed through traversing an Arctic state's waters (plus
likely stopping in an Arctic state port). And yet [the myth of an Arctic
wild west persists:]{.underline} "As a consequence of the rapidly
disappearing polar ice caps, there has been an [[increase in
unclaimed]{.mark} ocean and land [territory]{.mark}, beyond any nation's
control, that [countries]{.mark} are **[attempt]{.mark}**ing [to
claim]{.mark} jurisdiction over]{.underline}," proclaims one analysis.
[While this is a common argument, it [is **factually
incorrect**]{.mark}]{.underline}. It [is linking two separate issues
into one misleading causal chain: the decline in Arctic sea ice due to
climate change, and the ongoing process of defining boundaries and
spaces under international law.]{.underline} In fact, **[[there is no
unclaimed]{.underline}]{.mark}** land **[[territory in the
Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark}**. And [there is just one maritime boundary
disagreement, between the United States and Canada, over a tiny slice of
the Beaufort Sea]{.underline}.

Perhaps the "Great Arctic Race" is about the [[overlapping
claims]{.underline}]{.mark} to the central Arctic Ocean seabed. The U.N.
Convention on the Law of the Sea (UNCLOS) permits coastal states to
claim areas of the extended continental shelf beyond the 200 nautical
mile exclusive economic zone, if they can prove that these areas are a
natural prolongation of their continental shelf. Russia, Denmark (via
Greenland), and Canada have overlapping claims in the central Arctic.
Thus far, they [have]{.underline} [all [adhered to]{.mark} the process
of defining these claims in accordance with **[i]{.mark}**nternational
**[law]{.mark}**]{.underline}. **[Russia's law-abiding approach to the
Arctic shelf claims is a rare bright spot in the country's foreign
policy.]{.underline}**

The U.N. convention and seabed claims have been in the spotlight
recently as interest grows in seabed mining for critical minerals.
[Seabed [mining is not likely to drive conflict]{.mark} over Arctic
shelf claims]{.underline}. The [central [Arctic]{.mark}]{.underline}
Ocean --- over 200 nautical miles from any coast --- [[is]{.mark} also
an incredibly [inhospitable]{.mark} area for resource
extraction]{.underline}. The Arctic Ocean still freezes in the winter,
and is sunk in the frigid darkness of the polar night. The [seabed
mining]{.underline} industry, like most global industry, [is likely to
look for less costly options in warmer latitudes]{.underline}. While it
cannot be assumed that [[Russia]{.underline}]{.mark} will continue
pursuing its shelf claims in accordance with international law, it [is
likely to [use]{.mark} lower-cost [tools like lawfare
or]{.mark}]{.underline} [**[info]{.underline}**]{.mark}rmation [warfare
[to complicate]{.mark} central [Arctic]{.mark} Ocean [claims]{.mark},
[rather than]{.mark} apply high-cost and hard-to-sustain
[military]{.mark} options]{.underline}.

**[The [Myth of]{.mark} Increasing Accessibility and Arctic
[Shipping]{.mark}]{.underline}**

The [idea that climate change is increasing accessibility in the Arctic
is repeated so often that it is taken for granted]{.underline}. There is
an element of truth here --- the thick cap of multiyear Arctic sea ice
that has shaped the region for thousands of years is
[melting]{.underline} away and will soon be gone. But that [is just one
piece of a bigger puzzle]{.underline}, and the net [effect of
**[climate]{.mark} change in the Arctic [is making]{.mark} human
[activity harder, not easier]{.mark}**.]{.underline}

[[Shrinking]{.mark} sea [ice]{.mark} leaves coastlines [unprotected
from]{.mark} stronger, more frequent [storms]{.mark}. Coastal erosion is
higher in the Arctic than anywhere else, threatening U.S. radar
installations]{.underline}. [[Thawing]{.mark} permafrost causes
subsidence, [destroying infrastructure]{.mark} and saddling Arctic
states with massive costs]{.underline} --- highest in Russia. Permafrost
thaw will cause many more disasters in coming years, in Russia and
elsewhere. Massive [[wildfires]{.mark} further [endanger]{.mark}
communities and infrastructure in [the North]{.mark}]{.underline}. At
sea, increased storminess and stronger waves create hazards, along with
increases in drifting ice. [At the heart of it all lies
[uncertainty]{.mark} --- [a]{.mark} powerful [dissuading
factor.]{.mark}]{.underline}

[Arctic shipping lanes replacing Suez and Panama is another related
myth]{.underline}. When crisis erupts in the Red Sea, the Suez Canal, or
the Panama Canal, it is tempting to point north to emerging Arctic
shipping lanes as alternatives. [[On paper, Arctic shipping]{.mark}
routes appear to [offer]{.mark} considerable [savings]{.mark} of
distance, time, and fuel between northern ports in Asia, Europe, and
North America.]{.underline} However, **[[in practice,]{.mark} these
shipping [routes remain]{.mark} largely
[notional]{.mark}]{.underline}**. Over the last decade, [destinational
Arctic maritime activity has ticked up,]{.underline} mostly driven by
Russia's Yamal liquified natural gas project and expansion at Canada's
Mary River Mine. Container ships and roll-on roll-offs are barely
present, and this type of traffic has showed no change in the past
decade.

It is clear that [Canada has no interest in opening the Northwest
Passage]{.underline} to international shipping. Furthermore, the
[[complex routes]{.underline}]{.mark} of the passage [[have]{.mark}
draft [limitations]{.mark}]{.underline}, significant [remaining ice,
[and inadequate charting]{.mark}]{.underline}. [While]{.underline}
Russian President Vladimir [Putin]{.underline} [has been personally

imposing an ambitious timeline for cargo growth, [reality is not keeping
pace]{.underline}. The [route is plagued by its own draft limitations
and lack of supporting infrastructure, as well as unpredictable ice
hazards.]{.underline} International [shipping vanished following the
2022 invasion of Ukraine, and while cargo totals have rebounded and
grown in the past two years, the **[N]{.mark}**orthern **[S]{.mark}**ea
**[R]{.mark}**oute [is functionally]{.mark} a route [for]{.mark}
[Russian]{.mark}-flagged [ships]{.mark} of all sorts to carry oil and
gas to Asian markets]{.underline} (and bring in construction materials
for future developments, which have been weakened by sanctions).

The Myth of Russian Dominance and American Indifference

A large body of [mythmaking threatens to create a security
crisis]{.underline} in the Arctic [by sketching a 10-foot-tall Russia
intent on conquering the region]{.underline} and controlling the ocean
and casting the United States as an impotent weakling. These [outsize
legends are used to justify massive **spending increases in the
Arctic**,]{.underline} as well as significantly stronger measures
against Russia, both of which could **[contribute to
escalation]{.underline}** in the region [and also ripple across the
global balance]{.underline}.

[Russia's so-called "dominance" of the Arctic has been treated as
gospel.]{.underline} It is true that over half of the Arctic coastline
is Russian territory, and nearly half of the people living in the Arctic
live in Russia. And yet the discourse of Russian dominance in the Arctic
goes beyond geography. It also presents Russia's position as a threat to
the United States and to the entire Arctic Ocean. Of course, Moscow does
everything it can to nourish this sense of threat. Planting a flag at
the seabed of the North Pole --- possibly the most effective Russian
public relations stunt ever --- was a purely symbolic act, but continues
to serve as a reference point. Dropping paratroopers at the North Pole,
or unfurling a giant banner on the ice, are also gestures that feed the
legend.

[Arguments about Russia's so-called dominance of the Arctic reveal more
about America's own insecurities than the facts on the
ground]{.underline}. This framing creates a sense of unnecessary urgency
and relies on a set of measures that together would badly warp U.S.
strategic planning. Part of this is [the]{.underline} [legendary
**[icebreaker gap,]{.mark} thoroughly [debunked]{.mark}** by Paul Avey.
Does Russia dominate the Arctic with its icebreakers? Or does it just
have [a reasonable number]{.mark} of icebreakers [for its]{.mark} 24,000
kilometer Arctic [coastline]{.mark}, along which Putin is desperately
thirsting to build an international shipping lane that has so far failed
to catch on?]{.underline} [Sober analyses of Russia's power and strategy
in the Arctic need to be facts-based, rather than dealing in terms of
dominance.]{.underline}

Yes, [[Russian military]{.underline}]{.mark} [capabilities and
capacities [in the Arctic are]{.mark} substantive]{.underline}. It is
widely recognized that Russian leadership began to rebuild and expand
Arctic military capabilities and capacities as soon as it was able to,
starting in the early 2000s. [Most authoritative analyses note that
these installations and systems are primarily [defensive]{.mark} in
nature,]{.underline} [intended to help control and protect Russia's
economic and strategic interests in the Arctic,]{.underline} although
they could of course be useful in supporting aggression and, as
Katarzyna Zysk notes, Russian strategic thinking does not clearly
differentiate between offence and defense. [All great powers have
military bases along their coastlines, and Russia has both strategic and
economic interests to protect in its Arctic territory.]{.underline}

And if fears over Russia are not enough[, another myth holds that China
is coming for the Arctic]{.underline}. [Or that Chinese-Russian
cooperation will open the door for Chinese dominance in the
Arctic]{.underline}. As Marc Lanteigne has explained[, **[relations
between Beijing and Moscow in the Arctic are]{.mark}** far more
**[complicated]{.mark}**]{.underline}. And others have noted that the
myth of China's growing influence in the Arctic is mostly serving
Beijing's interests. It is important to watch the broader trajectory of
Russian-Chinese relations and not rush to assumptions about China's
future in the Arctic.

In parts as a result of fears over Russian dominance, claims that the
U.S. Department of Defense "needs to do more in the Arctic" have also
become commonplace. The United States has identified several critical
gaps in operational capabilities and capacities in the Arctic, including
in the National Strategy for the Arctic Region as well as the Department
of Defense's Arctic strategy. The most significant gap is the North
Warning System, and the ongoing effort to replace this critical system.
Canada has also underscored its commitment to upgrade the key
continental defense architecture, most recently in an Arctic-focused
defense policy update. But it's also important to note that the United
States has significant Arctic military capabilities, including the
highest concentration of fifth-generation aircraft in the world, as well
as an airborne division in Alaska, along with a North American Aerospace
Defense Command (NORAD) region. U.S. Navy submarines operate and train
regularly in the Arctic Ocean.

In the current budget context, in which the Department of Defense faces
flat and tight budgets, there are not free-floating resources to build
up any but the most vital capabilities in the Arctic. Broad calls for
significant spending increases in the Arctic are divorced from the
reality of spending needs in the Indo-Pacific and Europe, and from U.S.
national strategic priorities.

#### Greenland RE spurs export hub to EU and Asia

Tansu **Galimova** 20**24** **LUT** University, , Finland , ALSO - Rasul

[[is]{.underline}]{.mark} very [[vulnerable to oil

[[wind]{.underline}]{.mark} resources complementary
[[with]{.underline}]{.mark} [[solar]{.underline}]{.mark} resources [[may
enable a transition to a sustainable]{.underline}]{.mark} and
self-sufficient [[energy system]{.underline}]{.mark}. Greenland's

system between 2019 and 2050 [[and its]{.underline}]{.mark} position as

and South Korea]{.underline}]{.mark}, has been investigated in this
study using the EnergyPLAN model. The results [[indicate a 25% reduction
in annualised costs for a fully renewable energy
system]{.underline}]{.mark} compared to the reference system. Importing
regions can benefit from some of the lowest-cost energy carriers in the
world in 2030, and these energy carriers will continue to have a

for construction and operations. [[Renewable energy enables a full

enhances [[energy security]{.underline}]{.mark},
[[and]{.underline}]{.mark} provides opportunities for additional
[[export]{.underline}]{.mark} [[revenue]{.underline}]{.mark}s of up to
61 b€ annually.

#### Greenland exports enable global decarb

Tansu **Galimova** 20**24** **LUT** University, , Finland , ALSO - Rasul
Satymov, Dominik Keiner, Christian Breyer\"Sustainable energy transition
of Greenland and its prospects as a potential Arctic e-fuel and
e-chemical export hub for Europe and East Asia.\" Energy 286 (2024):
129605.
https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360544223029997
(ermo-MSDI, acc 6-23-2025)

[[Fossil fuels]{.underline}]{.mark} are the largest contributors to
climate change, [[account]{.underline}]{.mark}ing
[[for]{.underline}]{.mark} around [[75% of]{.underline}]{.mark} the
total emissions of [[greenhouse gases]{.underline}]{.mark} \[9\],
dominated by carbon dioxide (CO2). [[A transition to a 100% renewable
global energy system has been shown to be a
cost-effective]{.underline}]{.mark} measure to drastically cut
greenhouse gas emissions and meet the global 1.5◦C climate target
\[10,11\]. The transition is already undergoing as countries have
announced their own 100% renewable targets for the power sector \[12\]
and solar photovoltaics (PV) and wind power capacities are growing
rapidly year by year \[13\]. While the transition of the power sector is
straightforward, and direct electrification of some segments of
transport is undergoing with the uptake of battery electric vehicles
(BEV) \[14\], other sectors such as industry or long-range
transportation may have to rely on indirect electrification via
Power-to-X products \[15, 16\].

Studies have shown that [[many nations can meet their energy
demand]{.underline}]{.mark} for power, heat, transport, and industry
sectors [[with domestic]{.underline}]{.mark} renewable energy
([[RE]{.underline}]{.mark}) sources \[15\]. However, it may prove
significantly more costly [[in regions with less optimal renewable
resources]{.underline}]{.mark} compared to importing energy from regions
with better resource availability. Possibilities for [[e-hydrogen
exports from Norway]{.underline}]{.mark} \[17\], e-methane from
Australia \[18\] and South America \[19\], e-Fischer-Tropsch liquids
(e-FTL) from Patagonia \[20\], e-ammonia from Australia \[21\], or
e-methanol from Saudi Arabia, Chile, and Australia \[22\] [[have been
considered]{.underline}]{.mark}. Several studies have compared costs and
efficiencies of importing several renewable electricity-based fuels
(e-fuels) \[23\] and renewable electricity-based chemicals (e-chemicals)
\[24,25\]. While most studies focus on Germany or another European
country as an importer, the exporter portfolio is more diverse. Most
studies focus on the Maghreb region \[26\] and North Africa \[25,27\],
the Middle East \[23\], South America \[20,23,24\], or Australia
\[18,21,28\]. The trading links between 92 regions of the world were
analysed for e-methane, e-FTL, e-ammonia, and e-methanol \[29\].
Sub-Saharan Africa and South America were identified as the largest
exporters of e-fuels and e-chemicals, whereas [[Europe, Eurasia, and
Northeast Asia emerge as the main importers. Trading can reduce the
cost]{.underline}]{.mark} of e-fuels and e-chemicals by up [[to 38% in
these regions]{.underline}]{.mark}, indicating substantial benefits of
e-fuels and e-chemicals trading in the future \[29\]. Most exporter
regions identified in the studies are regions with excellent solar and
wind resources. [[However, Greenland has not been
covered]{.underline}]{.mark} in literature [[despite having
exceptionally good wind conditions]{.underline}]{.mark} enabling
low-cost wind electricity generation \[30\], and as a consequence
low-cost e-fuels and e-chemicals as found on the case of e-ammonia
\[31\].

In addition to emissions reduction, the
[[defossilisation]{.underline}]{.mark} of the energy system as the
primary source of emissions [[can]{.underline}]{.mark} bring about other
benefits such as [[improve]{.underline}]{.mark}d air [[quality and
energy security]{.underline}]{.mark}. Greenland is an autonomous Danish
dependent territory with a population of 56,000 people and an area of
almost 2.2 million km2 \[32\] and a nominal gross domestic product of
55,000 USD per capita \[33\]. The island provides an intriguing case for
study owing to its severe climate with year-round low temperatures,
favourable wind conditions, and areas that experience polar night during
winter and polar day during summer with the sun shining continuously for
24 h. Furthermore, the country's power sector is already mostly
defossilised with more than 80% of the total electricity generated from
hydropower \[34\]. Together [[with low population density and high land
availability, these untapped resources present the opportunity to
**defossilise all energy sectors**]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[and]{.underline}]{.mark} may even [[enable e-fuels and
e-chemicals]{.underline}]{.mark} production [[for the global
market]{.underline}]{.mark}.

#### Greenland ehub hedges global energy markets against geopolitical risks

Tansu **Galimova** 20**24** **LUT** University, , Finland , ALSO - Rasul
Satymov, Dominik Keiner, Christian Breyer\"Sustainable energy transition
of Greenland and its prospects as a potential Arctic e-fuel and
e-chemical export hub for Europe and East Asia.\" Energy 286 (2024):
129605.
https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360544223029997
(ermo-MSDI, acc 6-23-2025)

Despite a larger number of studies on the topic, [[Greenland
has]{.underline}]{.mark} been left out of the radars and no studies
exploring its potential as a fuel exporter were identified, except
numbers visualised in Fasihi et al. \[31\] on the case of e-ammonia.
However, [[excellent wind resources]{.underline}]{.mark} that [[can
offer low-cost renewable electricity]{.underline}]{.mark} \[30\] and a
vast availability of area may become important factors [[to consider
this location as a potential export hub]{.underline}]{.mark}. In this
research, the EnergyPLAN model has been utilised to offer a new
prospective on [[the Arctic region as a potential fuel production
hub]{.underline}]{.mark} that [[may help highly populated energy demand
centres]{.underline}]{.mark} to meet their climate goals. A connection
between Greenland and Europe through a sub-sea cable to export renewable
electricity has been previously considered \[87,88\]. One project has
been announced by H2Carrier and Anori to develop a 1.5 GW wind farm and
a floating green ammonia production vessel off the shore of Greenland
\[89\]. The idea of offshore synthetic fuel production on floating
islands has been introduced by Patterson et al. \[90\]. The present
study has shown that a large-scale production of e-fuels and e-chemicals
would be possible. e-Ammonia can be supplied at 81 €/MWhNH3 and 67
€/MWhNH3 in 2030 and 2050, respectively. Chances are high that e-fuels
business starts with e-ammonia, due to a lower complexity of its
production. e-Fuels and e-methanol can be supplied at 124 €/MWh and 106
€/MWh in the same periods. These costs are slightly higher than the
reported lowest e-ammonia production costs of 71 €/MWh and 53 €/MWh in
2030 and 2050 in the Atacama Desert, Horn of Africa, Yemen, and
southwest Niger \[31\]. Still, in 2030, [[e-ammonia can be produced in
Greenland for lower cost than in Europe, Japan, and South
Korea]{.underline}]{.mark}. The costs of e-fuels and e-methanol are at a
comparable level with the best sites in 2030, but are at a slightly
higher cost level in 2050 \[29,91\]. e-FTL can be produced at 130--160
€/MWh in best locations such as Chile, Somalia, or Australia in 2030 and
at 70--75 €/MWh in 2050, while e-methanol production costs range from
102 to 127 €/MWh in 2030 and 49--57 at best locations \[29\]. Purchasing
energy carriers from Greenland even at slightly higher cost may still be
beneficial for Europe where domestic e-fuel production may be limited.
However, [[business relations with a country associated with
EU]{.underline}]{.mark} as opposed to non-EU countries [[can have a
significantly higher]{.underline}]{.mark} impact on [[energy security
and may hedge against geopolitical risks]{.underline}]{.mark}.
Furthermore, even with lower production costs available in solar-rich
regions, imports from Greenland can help to diversify portfolios of
importers. North America has not been considered in this study as a
potential importer of e-fuels and e-chemicals from Greenland due to both
high land availability and high renewable resource potential for local
production. Furthermore, North America has been shown as a region with
almost no imports and small volumes of exports in 2030--2050 \[29\].

#### Arctic RE is cheaper than present import model

Tansu **Galimova** 20**24** **LUT** University, , Finland , ALSO - Rasul
Satymov, Dominik Keiner, Christian Breyer\"Sustainable energy transition
of Greenland and its prospects as a potential Arctic e-fuel and

(ermo-MSDI, acc 6-23-2025)

[[Climate change]{.underline}]{.mark} and the urgency of drastically
reducing greenhouse gas emissions [[necessitates rapid
defossilisation]{.underline}]{.mark} of the global energy system.
Regions in [[the]{.underline}]{.mark} fast-warming
[[Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark} are already suffering from the
consequences of climate change and their high dependence on oil
amplifies their vulnerability to oil price volatility. Greenland, which
is highly dependent on imported oil and already experiences substantial
climate impacts, [[need]{.underline}]{.mark}s [[to transform its energy
system. This novel study has shown technically feasible and economically
viable full sector energy transition pathways towards a 100% renewable
energy]{.underline}]{.mark} system.

This study has shown that excellent [[wind resources complemented by
solar]{.underline}]{.mark} photovoltaics during long summer days and
good hydropower resources [[can yield lower energy system
cost]{.underline}]{.mark}s for the residents, reduce greenhouse gas
emissions, and meet power, heat, transport, and industry sectors'
demand. Total annualised costs of an energy system based on 100%
renewable energy [[providing self-sufficiency is projected to be 25%
lower compared to the reference system]{.underline}]{.mark}, leading to
a reduction from 86.0 €/MWh to 72.6 €/MWh per unit of final energy. The
[[annualised investment costs required will be much
higher]{.underline}]{.mark}, growing from 38 m€ to 114 m€ between 2019
and 2050.
